		<title>New Research: #HowIWillChange Prevention Messages</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/11/new-research-howiwillchange-prevention-messages/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/11/new-research-howiwillchange-prevention-messages/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Tori Vandelinde]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 08 Nov 2018 23:47:14 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[#MeToo]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Journal of Interpersonal Violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social media]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/11/new-research-howiwillchange-prevention-messages/</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[&#8220;The fact that prevention strategies related to changing sexist and misogynistic language and behavior dominated Twitter discourse is promising&#8230;&#8221; In&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><em>&#8220;The fact that prevention strategies related to changing sexist and misogynistic language and behavior dominated Twitter discourse is promising&#8230;&#8221;</em></p>
<p>In the days after #MeToo started trending in October 2017, Twitter users responded with <a href="https://www.buzzfeed.com/bradesposito/how-i-will-change?utm_term=.paav46kg2#.open9X2N1" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">#HowIWillChange</a> asking men to prevent sexual violence and support survivors. A <a href="https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0886260518808854?rfr_dat=cr_pub%3Dpubmed&amp;url_ver=Z39.88-2003&amp;rfr_id=ori%3Arid%3Acrossref.org&amp;journalCode=jiva" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" class="broken_link">new study in the Journal of Interpersonal Violence</a> highlights the impacts of this trending hashtag.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" class=" wp-image-21532 alignleft" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/howiwillchange-300x164.png?x47887" alt="" width="293" height="160" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/howiwillchange-300x164.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/howiwillchange.png 700w" sizes="(max-width: 293px) 100vw, 293px" /><a href="https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2018-11/buso-btu110518.php" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Alyssa Harlow&#8217;s team with the Boston University School of Public Health</a> studied nearly 1,500 #HowIWillChange tweets. Over half of the tweets had primary prevention messages and another third were calling on each other to intervene when they see violence or hear misogynistic jokes or statements, to support survivors, and to hold those who cause harm accountable.</p>
<p>Not only is this Twitter conversation capable of creating lasting cultural shifts and social norms change, but the prevention strategies shared with #HowIWillChange also provide an excellent jumping off point for advancing prevention education. For example, in addition to role modeling positive behaviors for children, <a href="https://www.nsvrc.org/sites/default/files/2014-01/saam_2014_understanding-sexual-violence-tips-for-parents-and-caregivers.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parents can also talk with children of all ages about personal boundaries and healthy development</a>. Or, those who said they would examine their male privilege as their #HowIWillChange action can also read up on how<a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2017/12/power-privilege-and-prevention/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"> various forms of oppression (like racism, heterosexism, cissexism, etc.) contribute to high rates of violence</a>, and they can be vocal activists for intersectionality and sexual violence prevention in their peer circles.</p>
<img loading="lazy" class=" wp-image-21534 alignright" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/Screen-Shot-2018-11-08-at-2.29.25-PM-300x185.png?x47887" alt="" width="347" height="214" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/Screen-Shot-2018-11-08-at-2.29.25-PM-300x185.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/Screen-Shot-2018-11-08-at-2.29.25-PM.png 508w" sizes="(max-width: 347px) 100vw, 347px" />
<p>There were some tweets in opposition to #HowIWillChange and sexual violence prevention. These critiques illuminate which myths and misperceptions we can reframe and correct. Harnessing the momentum of Twitter trends can take our prevention efforts to new platforms and beyond our immediate communities. It is impossible to be comprehensive in our prevention efforts in 280 characters, so we must be strategic with Twitter and other social media. Berkeley Media Studies Group recently released <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/10/new-resources-on-prevention-communication-and-media-messaging/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">two prevention communication guides</a>&#8211;one with <a href="http://www.raliance.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">RALIANCE</a> and one with the <a href="https://www.nsvrc.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">National Sexual Violence Resource Center</a>&#8211;to help prevention practitioners craft effective, strategic prevention messages and communicate those messages with media outlets.</p>
<p>Read more about the study in a press release <a href="https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2018-11/buso-btu110518.php" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">here</a>, and to get information about the study, click <a href="https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0886260518808854?rfr_dat=cr_pub%3Dpubmed&amp;url_ver=Z39.88-2003&amp;rfr_id=ori%3Arid%3Acrossref.org&amp;journalCode=jiva" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" class="broken_link">here</a>.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Researchers discuss preventing sexual violence and engaging bystanders</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/04/researchers-discuss-preventing-sexual-violence-and-engaging-bystanders/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2018/04/researchers-discuss-preventing-sexual-violence-and-engaging-bystanders/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashleigh Klein-Jimenez]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Sun, 22 Apr 2018 22:31:18 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bystander intervention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[comprehensive prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=21141</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[An important conversation has occurred in the pages of the latest online edition of Violence Against Women exploring aspects in&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>An important conversation has occurred in the pages of the latest online edition of <a href="http://journals.sagepub.com/home/vaw" class="broken_link"><em>Violence Against Women</em></a> exploring aspects in the work to prevent sexual violence and specifically efforts to engage bystanders.</p>
<p>In the article titled,  &#8220;<a href="https://doi.org/10.1177/1077801217753322" class="broken_link">Bystander Training as Leadership Training: Notes on the Origins, Philosophy, and Pedagogy of the Mentors in Violence Prevention Model</a>,&#8221; Jackson Katz provides a comprehensive history of the long-running <a href="https://www.mvpstrat.com/">Mentors in Violence Prevention</a> (MVP) program. Katz also highlights the importance of social justice-oriented approaches to preventing sexual violence that seek to change harmful social norms and critiques bystander strategies that focus solely on interrupting individual events without incorporating gender transformative conversations within their trainings.</p>
<p>Over the years, engaging bystanders has become a common approach towar<span style="font-family: -apple-system, BlinkMacSystemFont, 'Segoe UI', Roboto, Oxygen-Sans, Ubuntu, Cantarell, 'Helvetica Neue', sans-serif;">d preventing sexual violence, especially on college campuses. There are numerous bystander intervention programs that at their core all seek to reduce incidence of sexual violence. Bystander intervention approaches have helped shift efforts that focus exclusively on perpetrators and survivors to efforts that elevate the role that every member of a community plays in prevention.</span></p>
<img loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-21142 alignright" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/Bystander-Intervention1-1-300x201.jpg?x47887" alt="" width="300" height="201" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/Bystander-Intervention1-1-300x201.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/Bystander-Intervention1-1.jpg 700w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" />
<p>Three response articles on Katz&#8217;s commentary and a <a href="https://doi.org/10.1177/1077801217753321" class="broken_link">final response from Katz</a> also appear in the journal.<a href="https://doi.org/10.1177/1077801217753320" class="broken_link"> Ann L. Coker</a>, <a href="https://doi.org/10.1177/1077801217753323" class="broken_link">Elizabeth Miller</a>, and <a href="https://doi.org/10.1177/1077801217753324" class="broken_link">Elizabeth Wilmerding, Mari Knuth-Bouracee and Jeffrey L. Edleson</a> authored these articles. The responses affirm the importance and place of social justice oriented efforts to prevent violence, while also raising important additional issues and questions. For example, Miller discusses how gender transformative programs challenge rigid norms of traditional masculinity making them a useful strategy to reduce sexual violence as well as homophobic bullying and sexual harassment. Miller says, &#8220;&#8230;the research on adolescent relationship abuse and sexual violence prevention point to key modifiable areas and approaches related to transforming gender norms, addressing homophobia, integrating with comprehensive sexuality education, and acknowledging the unique needs of youth already violence exposed.&#8221;  Another point, raised by Coker, is that research can assist prevention practitioners in determining what prevention efforts are successful. Coker points out the importance of using data to inform the prevention strategies and approaches that are being implemented and discusses the acceptance level of communities as a context for finding what works. Wilmderding, Knuth-Bouracee and Edleson advocate for a comprehensive approach to prevention with multiple messages that reinforce each other to change culture and prevent violence.</p>
<p>It is clear that sexual violence and related forms of violence are widespread, complex issues that require multiple prevention strategies and approaches. At the heart of prevention work is the need to change the factors that allow sexual violence to take place in the first place. Social justice seeks to address those factors and science provides us with the data and tools to get there. As we continue to learn more about what works to prevent violence, conversations such as this one between prevention practitioners, researchers, and advocates will remain necessary. Katz identifies the current social moment as a pivotal time to advance sexual violence prevention and this conversation is certainly part of that.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>CDC Advances Sexual Violence Prevention Research</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2016/10/cdc-advances-prevention-research/</link>

					<description><![CDATA[Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) has announced new research awards to continue building the evidence for preventing violence.&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p style="text-align: left;">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) has <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/CDC_Advances-Research-to-Prevent-Youth-Violence-SV-and-CA-and-Neglect_8-31-2016.pdf?x47887">announced</a> new research awards to continue building the evidence for preventing violence. Of the ten new research awards, five awards will evaluate the effectiveness of sexual violence primary prevention strategies from CDC’s <a href="https://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/rpe/" class="broken_link">Rape Prev</a><a href="https://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/rpe/" class="broken_link">ention Education (RPE)</a> programs. Awards were also given to advance research to prevent youth violence, and child abuse and neglect.</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/research_iStock_000015274873XSmall.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-20281 alignleft" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/research_iStock_000015274873XSmall-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="research_istock_000015274873xsmall" width="300" height="200" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/research_iStock_000015274873XSmall-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/research_iStock_000015274873XSmall.jpg 600w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a>The practice-based sexual violence primary prevention approaches that received research rewards include a strengths-based curriculum called the <a href="https://onecirclefoundation.org/TC.aspx" class="broken_link">Council for Boys and Young Men</a>, Peer Solutions’ youth-led violence prevention initiative <a href="http://www.peersolutions.org/stand-and-serve/">STAND &amp; SERVE</a>, a school-based program called <a href="https://sourcesofstrength.org/">Sources of Strength (SoS)</a>, an ongoing RPE-funded program called <a href="https://www.chsnc.org/educational-programs-and-training/youth-programs/wise-guys/" class="broken_link">Wise Guys: The Next Leve</a>l, and a community-level change strategy called <a href="http://yes.sph.umich.edu/">Youth Empowerment Solutions</a>.</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">These research awards will add to practice-based evidence of sexual violence prevention strategies and help close a critical gap in our understanding of what strategies best prevent sexual violence.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

					<description><![CDATA[Social media can be a great way to reach and communicate with teens, but anyone who&#8217;s tried to do it&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/Pew-2015.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-19560 alignleft" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/Pew-2015-242x300.png?x47887" alt="Pew 2015" width="242" height="300" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/Pew-2015-242x300.png 242w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/Pew-2015.png 319w" sizes="(max-width: 242px) 100vw, 242px" /></a>Social media can be a great way to reach and communicate with teens, but anyone who&#8217;s tried to do it can attest that the landscape of which social networks and apps the teens are interested in is constantly <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/01/pew_social_media_report/">changing</a>. It can be a huge challenge to keep up.</p>
<p>Pew Research Center released a report on their 2014-2015 nationally representative survey of teenagers aged 13-17 about their internet and social media use. The report has a lot of really useful information, including how app and network use varies by race, income, age, and more. Surprisingly (to me, anyway), Facebook is still at the top of the list of social networks for teenagers across the board.</p>
<p>The report, which you can find <a href="http://www.pewinternet.org/2015/04/09/teens-social-media-technology-2015/" target="_blank">here</a>, also has useful information on how often teenagers access the internet, the different devices they use, and more.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Sexual violence is a global problem</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/06/sexual-violence-is-a-global-problem/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/06/sexual-violence-is-a-global-problem/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 23 Jun 2015 17:28:21 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[global]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevalance]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19545</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[As prevention folks, we’re pretty familiar with the statistics about the prevalence of sexual violence. But most of the figures&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>As prevention folks, we’re pretty familiar with the statistics about the prevalence of sexual violence. But most of the figures we use, at least here in the US come from the US alone. How do other countries compare? Is there a lot of difference from country to country?</p>
<p>It’s not always an easy question to answer. Often, different countries use different surveys that make it hard to compare figures – that is, if a country collects data about sexual violence at all.</p>
<p>Earlier this month, the CDC’s Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report released a short article about sexual violence against children in seven low-income countries (Cambodia, Haiti, Kenya, Malawi, Swaziland, Tanzania, and Zimbabwe).</p>
<img loading="lazy" class=" wp-image-19546 aligncenter" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/06/highlighted-map-300x139.png?x47887" alt="highlighted map" width="524" height="242" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/06/highlighted-map-300x139.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/06/highlighted-map-1024x473.png 1024w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/06/highlighted-map.png 1236w" sizes="(max-width: 524px) 100vw, 524px" />
<p>It turns out that sexual violence is a major problem in most of these other countries, just as it is in the US. In five of the seven countries, at least 25% of women aged 18-24 had experienced some form of sexual violence before age 18. That figure varied more, from 6% of men in Cambodia to 21% of men in Haiti.</p>
<p>The study also has information about how many people who had experienced sexual violence as children received services, and more details about the different figures in different countries.</p>
<p>But the overall message is clear: sexual violence is a global problem.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation:</strong> Sumner, S. A. et al. (2015). Prevalence of sexual violence against children and use of social services – seven countries, 2007-2013. <em>Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report, 64</em>(21), 565-9.</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6421a1.htm?s_cid=mm6421a1_w" target="_blank">http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6421a1.htm?s_cid=mm6421a1_w</a></p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/06/sexual-violence-is-a-global-problem/feed/</wfw:commentRss>

		<item>
		<title>The basics of quality research</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/02/the-basics-of-quality-research/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/02/the-basics-of-quality-research/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 18 Feb 2015 17:16:41 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence informed practice]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19216</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[It can be really complicated to judge the quality of research studies. You can get deep into concerns about how&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>It can be really complicated to judge the quality of research studies. You can get deep into concerns about how a survey question was worded, how the timing of the evaluation or repetition of testing affected the responses people gave, how appropriate the data management and statistical analysis methods used were, and other nuanced, difficult questions. These questions are important, and are often addressed in reviews of multiple studies, but they’re not necessarily the kinds of questions I ask the first time I read a study.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" class="  wp-image-19217 alignright" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/magnifying-glass-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="magnifying glass" width="318" height="212" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/magnifying-glass-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/magnifying-glass-1024x682.jpg 1024w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/magnifying-glass.jpg 1280w" sizes="(max-width: 318px) 100vw, 318px" />And even reviews of studies are looking for certain components in studies that don’t necessarily have to do with the quality of the research. The CDC’s review of sexual violence prevention was looking for programs that were rigorously evaluated and demonstrated effectiveness in reducing violent behavior. In the whole fields of sexual and intimate partner violence prevention, only three programs and a federal policy fit these criteria, and we don’t even know exactly which aspects of those programs reduced violent behavior. And of course, these three programs are not necessarily appropriate for all communities.</p>
<p>We have a lot to learn from studies that don’t fit the criteria of the CDC’s review, or of other reviews. For instance, we can learn about risk and protective factors that are important for certain communities, strategies that are associated with reducing risk factors of perpetration, or interesting approaches or underlying principles for prevention programming. To do that, we need to be able to recognize which studies are stronger and which are weaker – which we should trust more, and which we should take with a grain (or a handful) of salt. It’s good to be able to read a study and get a general feel for whether you trust its conclusions or not. That’s what I do when I look through research studies and decide what to write about and what to say on PreventConnect.</p>
<p>I want to share what it is that I’m looking for when I read through a study. These are of the more important questions that I ask myself to decide how much I trust the study’s conclusions.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>How did they choose which people in the target population would be participants?</strong> Did they get an enrolment roster at a college and email a random selection of students? Or are they a professor who only did the program with their class? The first case is definitely better if there is a large enough sample and if the program participants are meant to be representative of the broader campus population. But the second case could be useful, too—maybe that professor could have done in-depth, qualitative interviews with those students that reveal a lot of nuance about their attitudes, barriers to performing the desired behaviors, what worked and what didn’t about a program, etc.—it’s just not likely to represent the whole student body.</li>
<li><strong>How are the program participants similar and different to my community? </strong>What are their ages? Linguistic background? Gender makeup? Educational status? Race? Do they live in urban or rural areas? Income level? Community values? Could a program done with middle school students also be done with high school students? Maybe, but maybe not. Maybe a program done in San Francisco could work in Dallas, Texas. But maybe not. <strong>Importantly, </strong>this question isn’t just about how different or similar the program population is from my community, but about how these differences matter for implementing the program. Many times I’ve read an abstract that seems interesting, only to find out that the study was done with 12 adolescents in Estonia or somewhere else I’m equally unfamiliar with. And it could be that the study findings are relevant in some ways to parts of the US, but I just don’t know enough about the similarities and differences to be confident one way or the other.</li>
<li><strong>What are the research questions asking? What are they not asking? </strong>Did the study ask about actual violent behaviors or behavioral intentions, or just knowledge, attitudes, and skills? Which risk and protective factors did the study address, and which did it leave out? How does that match up with the risk and protective factors that are important in my community, or with the goals and objectives of my program? Do the research questions fit my philosophy and understanding of the problems of sexual and/or intimate partner violence?</li>
<li><strong>If I’m trying to determine whether I trust a claim that a program was effective at creating some change…</strong>
<ul>
<li><strong>How many of the chosen/invited participants actually completed the program? </strong>If only 40% of the people invited participated, that can be a problem. What if the people who participated are those who are already interested in ending sexual and intimate partner violence? Their results from the program are probably going to be different than the results would have been for the other 60% of invited people. Also, if there was a comparison or control group, did a very different number of people drop out of the program group than the control group? Again, if a lot of people dropped out of the program group, I’m less likely to trust that the program was effective, even if it says it was in comparison to the control group. This is because if we had been able to measure change in the people who dropped out, we might find that they didn’t change. So considering everyone who did some part of the program, it could very well turn out that the program really didn’t make changes any differently than the control.</li>
<li><strong>What was the program compared to? </strong>Maybe there was no comparison or control group. If not, how do I know that any changes that occurred happened because of the program, and not because of some big local news story? (This might not be a problem for me. If I’m looking for innovative program activities or ways to adapt a program to a certain cultural context, I might be more interested in the content of the program than I am in being sure that the program caused certain changes.) If there <em>is</em> a comparison or control group, what activities did they do? Did they get a generic health education class, or did they just do the study without getting any sort of program? Are there different groups that got different combinations of program components (like Shifting Boundaries, where one group got a curriculum and a building intervention, one group got just a building intervention, one group got just a curriculum, and one group got nothing). Having a comparison or control group gives me more confidence that a program that says it was effective actually was.</li>
<li><strong>How was it decided which participants got which program components? </strong>This is getting nit-picky, but it is stronger if participants are randomly assigned to get the program or a control, rather than assigned based on logistics or other factors. But the reality is that most programs are given based on logistics, and it can get very expensive to have random assignment to different program groups, so I go easy on this one.</li>
<li><strong>How long after the program were post-tests administered (if there were pre- and post-tests)? </strong>The longer, the better. It’s not as important to me that a participant can answer a question correctly right after they participate in a class. I’m usually more interested in whether the knowledge, attitude, intentions, etc. stick with them over time.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><strong>If I’m trying to learn in-depth about program implementation, participants’ experience of a program, underlying principles for prevention, etc. in a qualitative (as opposed to quantitative) way…</strong>
<ul>
<li><strong>What are the researcher’s probable biases? </strong>Any good researcher will try to identify their biases and avoid letting them define their interpretation. But it’s good to think about it and check. Is this researcher trying to promote a certain approach over others? If so, how might this affect the way they interpret what the participants say?</li>
<li><strong>What is the researcher not saying? </strong>Qualitative studies almost always have a huge amount of data that researchers have to sift through and pick out the most profound, important pieces. What kinds of things does it seem like they might have glossed over?</li>
<li><strong>Do you agree with the researcher’s interpretation of the data?</strong> Ten different people can listen to the same set of interviews and draw ten different conclusions. You probably will only be able to see a little bit of the raw data, if any, but does what you see support what the researcher has claimed?</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>NIJ research: peer groups’ influence on teen dating violence</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/research-peer-groups-influence-on-teen-dating-violence/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/research-peer-groups-influence-on-teen-dating-violence/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 14 Jan 2015 20:58:05 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[peer influence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[teen dating violence]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19170</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[For teenagers, friends and peer groups have a stronger influence on attitudes and behavior than anyone else. Often, this is&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/NIJ-Research-TDV.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignright wp-image-19171" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/NIJ-Research-TDV-230x300.png?x47887" alt="NIJ Research TDV" width="221" height="287" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/NIJ-Research-TDV-230x300.png 230w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/NIJ-Research-TDV.png 605w" sizes="(max-width: 221px) 100vw, 221px" /></a>For teenagers, friends and peer groups have a stronger influence on attitudes and behavior than anyone else. Often, this is especially true when it comes to dating relationships. Teens’ peers can have huge influence over their decisions to start dating someone, to use violence or to build healthy relationships, to seek help or not if a dating partner is violent, and to stay in or end a relationship. If peers can have such a strong role in behaviors related to teen dating violence (TDV), it makes a lot of sense to target prevention efforts to certain risky peer networks or to use peer networks to reinforce the positive attitude, skill, and behavior changes that many prevention programs aim to make in individuals or in romantic relationships. It’s probably much harder for a teen to justify being violent in their relationship if all of their friends vocally disapprove of violence in romantic relationships and in general.</p>
<p><a href="https://www.ncjrs.gov/pdffiles1/nij/248337.pdf">Teen Dating Violence: How peers can affect risk &amp; protective factors</a> is a recent report by the National Institute of Justice. It explores how peer groups influence TDV and how prevention practitioners can work with peer groups to prevent it. In addition to discussing practical considerations for planning prevention efforts that target peer contexts, the report talks about four main points that research has made about why peer contexts are so important for preventing TDV.</p>
<ul>
<li>Having a risky peer context, for example where peers are victims and/or perpetrators of bullying, physical violence, and cyber bullying, increases the risk of victimization and/or perpetration of TDV. Having peers who are delinquent in school or the community is an especially important risk factor for TDV.</li>
<li>Teens who experience dating violence often seek help from their peers before anyone else. Peers can help or discourage victims from seeking further help.</li>
<li>Programs that have focused on social contexts have had some positive effects for preventing TDV. The building component of <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/shifting_boundaries/">Shifting Boundaries</a> shapes peer contexts, and was shown to reduce victimization and increase students’ intentions to intervene in violence. Another program that provided group treatment to girls in the child welfare system reduced TDV victimization.</li>
<li>Identifying risky behaviors or risk factors for TDV perpetration among individual students can also identify entire peer networks that might be at especially high risk for TDV perpetration, and thus might benefit from targeted prevention efforts. For example, if there is a high rate of bullying at a school, that school may be at higher risk for TDV. Or, if one or more teens in a certain friend group are delinquent at school, they and their entire friend group may be at higher risk for TDV perpetration.</li>
</ul>
<p>It certainly seems like peer contexts are important for TDV prevention. How have your prevention efforts worked specifically to change social and peer contexts? How can prevention efforts make peer network change a primary goal, and not just a side effect of individual change?</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p>Full citation: National Institute of Justice. (2014). <em>Teen dating violence: How peers can affect risk &amp; protective factors</em> (DOJ Publication no. NCJ 248337). Washington, DC: U.S. Department of Justice Office of Justice Programs.</p>
<p>Link: <a href="https://www.ncjrs.gov/pdffiles1/nij/248337.pdf">https://www.ncjrs.gov/pdffiles1/nij/248337.pdf</a></p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Research overview of engaging men and boys</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/research-overview-of-engaging-men-and-boys/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/research-overview-of-engaging-men-and-boys/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 12 Jan 2015 17:04:54 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[engaging boys]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[engaging men]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[gender]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Gender Transformative]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[global]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19155</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[I’ve written a lot about the Lancet series on Violence Against Women and Girls already, but I also really want to&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_19157" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19157" style="width: 200px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/cybergypsy/6488529663"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19157 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/feminist-man-200x300.jpg?x47887" alt="Source: Flickr Commons, Emma Campbell" width="200" height="300" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/feminist-man-200x300.jpg 200w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/feminist-man.jpg 427w" sizes="(max-width: 200px) 100vw, 200px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19157" class="wp-caption-text">Source: Flickr Commons, Emma Campbell</figcaption></figure>
<p>I’ve <a title="Prevention lessons from around the world" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/prevention-lessons-from-around-the-world/">written a lot</a> about the <a title="New research series on global violence against women and girls" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/11/new-research-series-on-global-violence-against-women-and-girls/">Lancet series</a> on Violence Against Women and Girls already, but I also really want to highlight the piece they did on working with men and boys. The article by Jewkes, Flood, and Lang is a great introductory or background piece to engaging men and boys, because it talks about the historical context of involving men in prevention, about why we want to engage men and boys, and about what strategies and approaches we should use to try to engage men today.</p>
<p>The article starts with a history of men’s involvement in prevention. Understanding this historical context is crucial for being able to understand men’s current efforts to prevent violence against women and girls. After describing this context, the article goes on to give a lot of detail about what efforts to engage men and boys in prevention can and should look like. As the <a title="Prevention lessons from around the world" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/prevention-lessons-from-around-the-world/">lessons from prevention</a> told us, prevention work should focus on gendered power dynamics. This article gives us a good deal more information on how to do that. The main recommendation is to base efforts to engage men and boys in gender theory, both as an ideological foundation and as a guide for specific program objectives.</p>
<p>The ideas of gender theory that the article emphasizes are that gender is socially constructed, and that the dominant ideal of masculinity isn’t the only or the best kind of masculinity. The dominant ideal often involves men being tough and strong and having control and power over women, girls, and other men and boys. This connects to violence against women and girls because, “although not all men are violent, and some actively oppose violence, the use of violence over women is one source of power accorded to men in many settings.” People can be more or less able to live up to their view of gender ideals based on their socio-economic status, employment status, traumatic events, and other life circumstances. Finally, gender is relational, meaning that gender ideals, identity, and expression depend on other women and men’s expectations and approval.</p>
<p>Based on these ideas, the article suggests that the goals of prevention work with men and boys should be to transform gender ideals, expectations, and expression. The work should highlight variety in the kinds of masculinity that exist in a community, inconsistency in gender ideals, fluctuation of gender expression over time and in different circumstances, and similarities between men and women. It should also fight homophobia and reveal and challenge men’s privilege. These ideas challenge the dominant gender ideals and make room for individual men and boys to become more comfortable rejecting violent and controlling masculinity, and eventually collectively change gender norms in their community.</p>
<p>To me, the key is to not just talk about what masculinity is, why it’s connected to violence, and how it harms women, girls, men, and boys, but to figure out concrete ways to challenge men’s privilege, change what masculinity means, and create space for more variety in gender expression. A lot of programs do a good job of accomplishing these goals for individuals, but it’s harder to translate those changes to entire communities and societies. How does your work with men affect these higher levels? How can we make more community and societal change in gender expectations and ideals?</p>
<p>You can read the entire article for free at the link below. Just create a free username, and you’ll be granted access.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Full citation:</strong> Jewkes, R., Flood, M., &amp; Lang, J. (2014). From work with men and boys to changes of social norms and reduction of inequities in gender relations: A conceptual shift in prevention of violence against women and girls. <em>Lancet</em>. Advance online publication. doi: 10.1016/S0140-6736(14)61683-4</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(14)61683-4/fulltext">http://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(14)61683-4/fulltext</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract:</strong></p>
<blockquote><p>“Violence perpetrated by and against men and boys is a major public health problem. Although individual men&#8217;s use of violence differs, engagement of all men and boys in action to prevent violence against women and girls is essential. We discuss why this engagement approach is theoretically important and how prevention interventions have developed from treating men simply as perpetrators of violence against women and girls or as allies of women in its prevention, to approaches that seek to transform the relations, social norms, and systems that sustain gender inequality and violence. We review evidence of intervention effectiveness in the reduction of violence or its risk factors, features commonly seen in more effective interventions, and how strong evidence-based interventions can be developed with more robust use of theory. Future interventions should emphasise work with both men and boys and women and girls to change social norms on gender relations, and need to appropriately accommodate the differences between men and women in the design of programmes.”</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/research-overview-of-engaging-men-and-boys/feed/</wfw:commentRss>
			<slash:comments>1</slash:comments>
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">19155</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>The role of alcohol policy in prevention</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/the-role-of-alcohol-policy-in-prevention/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2015/01/the-role-of-alcohol-policy-in-prevention/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 08 Jan 2015 17:07:40 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[alcohol]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[policy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19160</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Efforts to reduce alcohol consumption can be controversial when it comes to sexual violence prevention. So let me start with&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_19161" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19161" style="width: 256px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/froderik/5966641707"><img loading="lazy" class=" wp-image-19161" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/alcohol-bottles-300x199.jpg?x47887" alt="Source: Flickr Commons, Fredrik Rubensson" width="256" height="170" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/alcohol-bottles-300x199.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/alcohol-bottles.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 256px) 100vw, 256px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19161" class="wp-caption-text">Source: Flickr Commons, Fredrik Rubensson</figcaption></figure>
<p>Efforts to reduce alcohol consumption can be controversial when it comes to sexual violence prevention. So let me start with a few important points. Alcohol does not cause sexual violence. Alcohol is associated with some factors that can be associated with sexual violence perpetration, but alcohol consumption doesn’t lead to sexual violence in a vacuum. Alcohol may lower inhibitions, decrease empathy, and cause problems with judgment, but these things don’t lead to sexual violence in the absence of factors like sexual entitlement, sexual objectification, victim-blaming or rape-accepting attitudes, and a desire for power and control.</p>
<p>A new study came out that PreventConnect will soon be giving a web conference about. The study, by Lippy &amp; DeGue, looks at the potential effects that alcohol-related policies can have on sexual violence perpetration. The article does an excellent job of contextualizing the role of alcohol policy by acknowledging and describing the limitations that I just mentioned. But it also makes a good public health case for focusing on how alcohol policy can help in prevention. It’s true that alcohol consumption isn’t a root cause of sexual violence perpetration, but it does have an impact on perpetration in many populations, including a wide range of ages and people in both different- and same-sex relationships. According to the article, alcohol consumption by the perpetrator, victim, or both is a factor in about half of all sexual assaults, and about one-third to three-fourths of perpetrators had been drinking at the time of the assault. So from a public health standpoint, it makes a lot of sense to attempt to reduce alcohol consumption as a sort of quicker fix to prevent perpetration. It won’t eradicate gender inequality, patriarchy, or sexual objectification or increase empathy and healthy relationships, but it might prevent some sexual assaults in the meantime. That makes it a potentially important part of a comprehensive approach to sexual violence prevention.</p>
<p>So what did the study find? As a review of many studies related to alcohol and sexual violence, the article mostly showed that there needs to be more research related specifically to alcohol policy’s effects on sexual violence, but it also highlighted some strategies that show promise.</p>
<ul>
<li>Increasing alcohol prices through higher taxes or through prohibiting drink specials and promotions shows consistent effects of reducing sexual violence perpetration. More research is needed on policies restricting or prohibiting happy hours, which may encourage binge drinking.</li>
<li>Policies restricting the times when alcohol can be sold (like closing time laws) have mixed results in their effects on violence, but they have been shown to reduce alcohol consumption.</li>
<li>Higher alcohol outlet density has been linked to higher rates of sexual violence victimization, as well as higher rates of assault and intimate partner violence. Alcohol outlet density is the number of outlets (businesses) that sell alcohol either in a certain geographic area or compared to the population (e.g. 3 outlets on a city block, 2 outlets per 100 people living in a city, etc.). Alcohol outlet density can be regulated using alcohol sale licensing policy.</li>
<li>We need a lot more research on policies to reduce over-serving customers in bars or restaurants, but there’s some preliminary evidence that these types of policies can reduce assault and physical aggression.</li>
<li>There’s no good evidence on policies regarding placement or content of alcohol marketing, but there is evidence that being exposed to sexist alcohol marketing is related to sexual violence perpetration.</li>
<li>Policies banning alcohol on college campuses or in specific residence halls are associated with reduced sexual violence victimization. But this effect depends on the availability of alcohol in the community surrounding a campus, so policies that affect both colleges and their communities should be considered.</li>
</ul>
<p>Creating changes at the societal level to prevent sexual violence is notoriously difficult to do, and we have very little evidence for what kinds of societal-level efforts can actually reduce sexual violence perpetration. Alcohol-related policies may not address the causes of sexual violence perpetration, but it looks like they can still reduce sexual violence rates. It’s a part of the solution that definitely needs to be explored further.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Full citation: </strong>Lippy, C. &amp; DeGue, S. (2014). Exploring alcohol policy approaches to prevent sexual violence perpetration. <em>Trauma, Violence, &amp; Abuse</em>. Advance online publication. doi: 10.1177/1524838014557291</p>
<p><strong>Link: </strong><a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/25403447">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/25403447</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract: </strong></p>
<blockquote><p>“Sexual violence continues to be a significant public health problem worldwide with serious consequences for individuals and communities. The implementation of prevention strategies that address risk and protective factors for sexual violence at the community level are important components of a comprehensive approach, but few such strategies have been identified or evaluated. The current review explores one potential opportunity for preventing sexual violence perpetration at the community level: alcohol policy. Alcohol policy has the potential to impact sexual violence perpetration through the direct effects of excessive alcohol consumption on behavior or through the impact of alcohol and alcohol outlets on social organization within communities. Policies affecting alcohol pricing, sale time, outlet density, drinking environment, marketing, and college environment are reviewed to identify existing evidence of impact on rates of sexual violence or related outcomes, including risk factors and related health behaviors. Several policy areas with initial evidence of an association with sexual violence outcomes were identified, including policies affecting alcohol pricing, alcohol outlet density, barroom management, sexist content in alcohol marketing, and policies banning alcohol on campus and in substance-free dorms. We identify other policy areas with evidence of an impact on related outcomes and risk factors that may also hold potential as a preventative approach for sexual violence perpetration. Evidence from the current review suggests that alcohol policy may represent one promising avenue for the prevention of sexual violence perpetration at the community level, but additional research is needed to directly examine effects on sexual violence outcomes.”</p></blockquote>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Prevention lessons from around the world</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/prevention-lessons-from-around-the-world/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/prevention-lessons-from-around-the-world/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 18 Dec 2014 17:00:24 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[global]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[practice]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>

					<description><![CDATA[Two of my favorite things about the recent series on Violence Against Women and Girls in The Lancet are that&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>Two of my favorite things about the recent <a title="New research series on global violence against women and girls" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/11/new-research-series-on-global-violence-against-women-and-girls/">series</a> on Violence Against Women and Girls in The Lancet are that they focus on prevention around the world&#8211;including efforts from low- and middle-income countries that often don’t get a lot of attention in the US&#8211;and that there is a really strong emphasis in the series on lessons learned from practice, not just from research studies. These two factors come together in “Prevention of violence against women and girls: lessons from practice” to shed some new light on what might work to prevent violence against women and girls. The article essentially summarizes some of the most important trends, successes, and challenges to shed new light on what might work in prevention.</p>
<figure style="width: 375px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="http://www.thelancet.com/cms/attachment/2020894921/2041097832/gr2.jpg"><img loading="lazy" class="" src="http://www.thelancet.com/cms/attachment/2020894921/2041097832/gr2.jpg" alt="" width="375" height="425" /></a><figcaption class="wp-caption-text">Michau et al.&#8217;s socio-ecological model (2014).</figcaption></figure>
<p>To me, the take-away messages from the article are about addressing the root causes and inequalities that lead to violence against women and girls, thinking big, collaborating to increase impact, and being innovative.</p>
<p>A major point was that it’s crucial to maintain a focus on gender inequity and power dynamics in interpersonal relationships, communities, and societies. I think there’s sometimes a strong impulse to de-politicize prevention work by skirting issues about patriarchy, gender socialization, or other forms of oppression in order to reach broader audiences. But this article puts it plainly: “Simply stated, to be born a girl in a patriarchal society is a fundamental risk factor for various types of gender-based violence.”</p>
<p>Like much of the guidance produced in the US, this article encourages working across the socio-ecological model, and it provides some interesting ideas that we might not always consider. First of all, they included a diagram with suggestions for how to intervene at different levels that are different from the suggestions we often see in the US. They also discuss some particulars about how to focus on each level. Small-scale workshops can make significant change for individuals, but violence against women and girls is such a large-scale problem that we really need to do more. Awareness raising is also important for changing community norms, but they need to have explicit goals and content to change attitudes, norms, and behaviors, rather than just sharing statistics.</p>
<p>The article suggests that in order to really make change at the community and society levels, there has to be strong cross-sector collaboration. It encourages collaborations with policymakers, the justice system, the health care sector, and educational systems (at the organizational and policy level, not just in classrooms), as well as closer partnership between prevention researchers and practitioners. The root problems of oppression and gender inequity affect all of these sectors, and the solutions should involve them all, too.</p>
<p>Finally, the article talks about the importance of innovation. Even considering some strong programming from low- and middle-income countries, the existing evidence base is small. Some interventions in other countries have led to strong involvement of community leaders, changes in gender norms and expressions, and changes in interpersonal relationships. But we still don’t know how well those kinds of interventions will work here. Perhaps more important than replicating those programs in other countries is to be inspired by the way these programs did things differently, and to think critically about why we do what we do in our prevention programming. Thoughtful analysis of each community’s risk and protective factors might suggest doing something totally different than school-based educational sessions and a social marketing campaign. So what could work better? How can we innovate to find more strategies that create change?</p>
<p>You can read more about some innovative programs in other countries and specific recommendations for work at each level of the socio-ecological model for free at the link below. Just create a free username, and you’ll be able to access the article.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Full citation</strong>: Michau, L., Horn, J., Bank, A., Dutt, M., &amp; Zimmerman, C. (2014). Prevention of violence against women and girls: Lessons from practice. <em>Lancet</em>. Advance online publication. doi:10.1016/S0140-6736(14)61797-9</p>
<p><strong>Link: </strong><a href="http://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(14)61797-9/fulltext">http://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(14)61797-9/fulltext</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract:</strong></p>
<blockquote><p>This Series paper describes programming to prevent violence against women and girls, and emphasises the importance of systematic, sustained programming across the social ecology (ie, the delicate equilibrium of interacting social, institutional, cultural, and political contexts of people’s lives) to transform gender-power inequalities. Effective prevention policy and programming is founded on five core principles: first, analysis and actions to prevent violence across the social ecology (individual, interpersonal, community, and societal); second, intervention designs based on an intersectional gender-power analysis; third, theory-informed models developed on the basis of evidence; fourth, sustained investment in multisector interventions; and finally, aspirational programming that promotes personal and collective thought, and enables activism on women’s and girls’ rights to violence-free lives. Prevention programming of the future will depend on all of us having a vision of, and a commitment to, gender equality to make violence-free lives for women and girls a reality.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Revisión de investigación sobre la prevención de la violencia en el noviazgo</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/revision-de-investigacion-sobre-la-prevencion-de-la-violencia-en-el-noviazgo/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/12/revision-de-investigacion-sobre-la-prevencion-de-la-violencia-en-el-noviazgo/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 03 Dec 2014 20:35:46 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Featured]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[dating violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[español]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Spanish]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19137</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Mucha de la investigación sobre la violencia en el noviazgo se efectúa en inglés, y hasta ahora, todas las revisiones&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_19138" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19138" style="width: 237px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/suttonhoo22/305806118"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19138 " src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/investigacion-300x199.jpg?x47887" alt="Source/Fuente: Flickr Commons, Dayna Bateman" width="237" height="157" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/investigacion-300x199.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/12/investigacion.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 237px) 100vw, 237px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19138" class="wp-caption-text">Source/Fuente: Flickr Commons, Dayna Bateman</figcaption></figure>
<p>Mucha de la investigación sobre la violencia en el noviazgo se efectúa en inglés, y hasta ahora, todas las revisiones y meta-análisis de esta investigación eran en inglés también. Investigadores en Colombia recientemente publicaron <a href="http://www.redalyc.org/articulo.oa?id=80131179008">una revisión</a> de estudios en inglés y en español de programas de prevención de la violencia en el noviazgo que se realizaron entre 1990 y 2012. Entre los 13 estudios revisados, 10 fueron en inglés, y los 3 en español se llevaron a cabo en España, México y Chile. Todos los programas en esta revisión, incluyendo los programas en español, se desarrollaron en Estados Unidos o en Canadá. No obstante los programas en español son relativamente recientes, así que podría ser que el interés en la prevención de la violencia en el noviazgo esta creciendo en países hispanohablantes. Si es así, el cuerpo de investigación en español probablemente va a aumentarse también.</p>
<p>Todos los programas identificados intentan a educar y entrenar a estudiantes adolescentes para mejorar sus conocimientos, actitudes, habilidades y comportamientos relacionados con el noviazgo. Los programas consisten en clases de 5-21 sesiones que tienen lugar en las escuelas.</p>
<p>La calidad de los resultados de los estudios no es ideal, ya que los programas varían mucho en sus métodos, duraciones, medidas y tamaños de muestra, y la mayoría de los estudios no hicieron medidas de seguimiento. Sin embargo, generalmente los programas lograron mejorar los conocimientos y actitudes de los estudiantes aunque no lograron cambiar sus comportamientos. Desafortunadamente los participantes de todos los programas fueron estudiantes adolescentes, así que no se puede generalizar los resultados a otras poblaciones como los estudiantes universitarios o los adultos.</p>
<p>La revisión recomienda que los programas de prevención de la violencia en el noviazgo se enfoquen en cambiar comportamientos, no solamente conocimientos y actitudes, que consistan en métodos aparte de enseñanza en escuelas, que mejoren las metodologías de evaluación y que tengan participantes más diversos.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Cita completa</strong>: Martínez Gómez, J.A.&amp; Rey Anacona, C.A.. (2014). Prevención de violencia en el noviazgo: Una revisión de programas publicados entre 1990 y 2012. <em>Pensamiento Psicólogo, 12</em>(1), 117-132. doi:10.11144/Javerianacali.PPSI12-1.pvnr</p>
<p><strong>Enlace:</strong> <a href="http://www.redalyc.org/articulo.oa?id=80131179008">http://www.redalyc.org/articulo.oa?id=80131179008</a></p>
<p><strong>Resumen:</strong></p>
<blockquote><p>“Objetivo. Se busca identificar, describir y analizar los componentes, metodología y resultados de los programas dirigidos a prevenir la violencia en el noviazgo, reseñados en artículos recuperados de las bases de datos Redalyc, Psicodoc, Science Direct, Proquest y Google académico, publicados entre enero de 1990 y noviembre de 2011. Método. Del total de artículos revisados, solo 13 cumplieron con el criterio de selección de describir la evaluación empírica de este tipo de programas, entre los que destaca el uso del entrenamiento en manejo de conflictos, habilidades para resolución de problemas, conocimientos sobre relaciones saludables, acoso / asalto sexual, cambio de actitudes sobre la violencia en el noviazgo y entrenamiento en habilidades de búsqueda de ayuda. Resultados. En general, estos artículos reportan una disminución en actitudes violentas e incremento del conocimiento sobre la violencia, aunque la mayoría no realizó una evaluación de cambio conductual y en los pocos en donde se hicieron mediciones de seguimiento, no se reporta el mantenimiento de los logros. Conclusión. Se hace necesario, para el avance del conocimiento sobre el tema, realizar estudios con una metodología más rigurosa, centrados en el cambio efectivo de conductas y mejorar la capacidad de generalización de estos estudios a poblaciones diferentes a adolescentes y adultos jóvenes escolarizados.”</p></blockquote>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<pubDate>Tue, 25 Nov 2014 18:18:28 +0000</pubDate>

					<description><![CDATA[The Lancet, one of the top medical journals in the world, has released a series of articles on global efforts against&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_19126" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19126" style="width: 276px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/ahinsajain/3689780385/in/photolist-6C46ac-pac1RH-5T6yWU-jgpJ18-oTsei8-4vM6aK-6uoXUU-4jdaRF-7bPPFS-aqQATx-7bN3uZ-cBBwsC-fNyn5S-8KFqSQ-7cda1y-fqqiVg-5uW3UR-p5PQbr-76g3vV-4jd7gD-6C5zAv-6DiC3n-6C5Lyg-6EK5A6-4Eyin3-oTuPRF-iWk3xR-dvoipB-5CcCAg-pBeWLA-j1jRUn-9FdGJY-gUSRca-gF9N97-iWiz4r-hi8VK4-iWntvW-iWntE3-iWiCix-5T2a7M-6izhAu-iWk3aB-7xKqAj-kZS36e-njzUst-7bQTCo-7bMgeB-apr84K-o7QadM-4EypD7/" class="broken_link"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19126 " src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/11/3689780385_e07dfe4cc6_z-300x199.jpg?x47887" alt="Source: Flickr Creative Commons, rajkumar1220" width="276" height="183" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/11/3689780385_e07dfe4cc6_z-300x199.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/11/3689780385_e07dfe4cc6_z.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 276px) 100vw, 276px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19126" class="wp-caption-text">Source: Flickr Creative Commons, rajkumar1220</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Lancet, one of the top medical journals in the world, has released a <a href="http://www.thelancet.com/series/violence-against-women-and-girls" target="_blank">series of articles</a> on global efforts against violence against women and girls. The series includes five reviews of approaches to responding to violence against women, an audio file of a discussion of the series, and comments from experts. You can register with the Lancet for free, and then see the full articles in the series at no charge.</p>
<p>The series focuses on low- and middle-income countries&#8217; efforts in addition to studies from the US and other high-income countries. This is really exciting to me because a lot of low- and middle-income countries have a stronger focus on prevention than we do here, and I think there&#8217;s a lot we can learn from their work. I&#8217;ll be posting more about my take on some of the articles in the series soon.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Bullying, homophobic teasing, and sexual harassment</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/11/bullying-homophobic-teasing-and-sexual-harassment/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/11/bullying-homophobic-teasing-and-sexual-harassment/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 03 Nov 2014 17:14:27 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bullying]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bullying prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[homophobia]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[risk factors]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Sexual Harassment]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19094</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Bullying prevention has been a major focus for schools for the past several years, and it can be a good&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_19095" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19095" style="width: 268px" class="wp-caption alignleft"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/53771866@N05/7040908861/in/photolist-7PzYFL-6o4zMA-4Q4Lvn-5fqy8i-bJbuUn-874TSo-bz5ddC-66WK4q-bMYMy2-bz5dSm-8qhRSN-5ZZxpm-9A4MEw-5fThkc-74cEbC-ejY84e-4NYsuE-8W95ob-eUHnE5-eEtxAj/"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19095" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/7040908861_f56d66f8b5_z-300x222.jpg?x47887" alt="Source: Flickr Commons, Twentyfour Students" width="268" height="198" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/7040908861_f56d66f8b5_z-300x222.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/7040908861_f56d66f8b5_z.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 268px) 100vw, 268px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19095" class="wp-caption-text">Source: Flickr Commons, Twentyfour Students</figcaption></figure>
<p>Bullying prevention has been a major focus for schools for the past several years, and it can be a good entry point for sexual and dating violence prevention efforts to get into schools. Over the years, there has been <a title="Links between Bullying &amp; Sexual Violence: Possibilities for Prevention" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/02/links-between-bullying-sexual-violence-possibilities-for-prevention/" target="_blank">more</a> and <a title="New research: Does bullying lead to dating aggression?" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-does-bullying-lead-to-dating-aggression/" target="_blank">more</a> support for the “bullying – sexual violence pathway,” a theory that children who bully other children are more likely to perpetrate sexual violence, like sexual harassment, unwanted touching/groping, and maybe even more severe sexual violence, as well as teen dating violence.</p>
<p>New research by Dr. Dorothy Espelage et al. adds another dimension to this pathway that can be important for prevention. They followed almost 1,000 fifth-seventh graders for two years and measured their bullying perpetration and homophobic teasing perpetration as they related to later sexual harassment perpetration. Preventing sexual harassment is a really important goal, since according to <a title="Sexual Harassment Victimization and Perpetration Among High School Students" href="http://vaw.sagepub.com/content/20/10/1203.abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">another recent study</a>, about <strong>1 in 8 boys</strong> and <strong>1 in 20 girls</strong> perpetrate sexual harassment in high school. The study found that girls who bullied others at the beginning of the study were over <strong>3</strong> times more likely to sexually harass others two years later, but homophobic teasing didn’t affect this relationship. Boys who bullied others were over <strong>4.5</strong> times more likely to perpetrate sexual harassment than boys who didn’t bully, and boys who engaged in homophobic teasing were <strong>1.66</strong> times more likely to perpetrate sexual harassment than boys who didn’t. Interestingly, if boys engaged in a lot of bullying and a lot of homophobic teasing, they were even more likely to engage in sexual harassment two years later than boys who did both bully and engage in homophobic teasing, but did so rarely.</p>
<p>Clearly, preventing bullying can have a big impact on preventing sexual harassment for both boys and girls. But it looks like it can be important to also focus especially on preventing homophobic teasing by boys. Not only would this focus make a stronger connection between bullying and sexual violence, but it also supports our goals of preventing violence against all people, regardless of gender or sexual orientation, and of challenging oppressive gender roles. If boys can be free from homophobic bullying or gender policing, maybe they’ll be less likely to be hostile toward people and traits they deem feminine and more likely to be openly caring, sensitive, and empathetic.</p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation:</strong> Espelage, D.L., Basile, K.C., De La Rue, L., &amp; Hamburger, M.E. (2014). Longitudinal associations among bully, homophobic teasing, and sexual violence perpetration among middle school students. <em>Journal of Interpersonal Violence</em>. Advance online publication. doi:10.1177/0886260514553113</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/10/10/0886260514553113.abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/10/10/0886260514553113.abstract</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract: </strong></p>
<blockquote><p>“Bullying perpetration and sexual harassment perpetration among adolescents are major public health issues. However, few studies have addressed the empirical link between being a perpetrator of bullying and subsequent sexual harassment perpetration among early adolescents in the literature. Homophobic teasing has been shown to be common among middle school youth and was tested as a moderator of the link between bullying and sexual harassment perpetration in this 2-year longitudinal study. More specifically, the present study tests the Bully–Sexual Violence Pathway theory, which posits that adolescent bullies who also participate in homophobic name-calling toward peers are more likely to perpetrate sexual harassment over time. Findings from logistical regression analyses (n = 979, 5th-7th graders) reveal an association between bullying in early middle school and sexual harassment in later middle school, and results support the Sexual Harassment Victimization and Perpetration Among High School Students Bully–Sexual Violence Pathway model, with homophobic teasing as a moderator, for boys only. Results suggest that to prevent bully perpetration and its later association with sexual harassment perpetration, prevention programs should address the use of homophobic epithets.”</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/11/bullying-homophobic-teasing-and-sexual-harassment/feed/</wfw:commentRss>
