		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">19094</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>How researchers adapted an evidence-based program</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/how-researchers-adapted-an-evidence-based-program/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/how-researchers-adapted-an-evidence-based-program/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 29 Oct 2014 17:47:32 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[adaptation]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[cult]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence based practice]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19090</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[We talk a lot about how using “one-size-fits-all” programs or “programs in a box” is probably not the best way&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>We talk a lot about how using “one-size-fits-all” programs or “programs in a box” is probably not the best way to prevent sexual and intimate partner violence in all of our diverse communities. An evidence-based program that was designed for mostly-white middle schoolers in a suburban area, for example, probably won’t be that effective for ethnically diverse college students in an urban area. But there’s also a strong trend toward doing prevention work that’s <a title="Understanding Evidence: New online tool for making decisions about prevention" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/03/understanding-evidence-new-online-tool-for-making-decisions-about-prevention/">based in evidence</a> – <a title="Understanding Evidence" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2011/09/understanding-evidence/">experiential, contextual, and research-based</a>.</p>
<p><img loading="lazy" class="alignright wp-image-19091 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/adaptation-300x198.png?x47887" alt="adaptation" width="300" height="198" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/adaptation-300x198.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/adaptation.png 876w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" />What I think is the most difficult part of planning evidence-based programming is bridging experiential and contextual evidence with research evidence. Even if we have a really <a title="Culturally grounded prevention to create change" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/culturally-grounded-prevention-to-create-change/">deep understanding of our communities</a>, it’s not necessarily easy to figure out how to use that understanding to make research-based programs fit.</p>
<p>A new <a href="http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/10/01/0886260514552278.abstract" class="broken_link">research article</a> by Vangie Foshee and others talks about <strong>how they went about adapting</strong> the <em>Families for Safe Dates</em> (FSD) program, which aims to give parents and their adolescent children a structured guide for talking about preventing teen dating violence. The adaptation of the program, called <em>Mothers and Teens for Safe Dates</em> (MTSD), was tailored to mothers who had left abusive relationships and their adolescents.</p>
<p>First, the researchers used logic and theory. There were a lot of elements in FSD that they didn’t think would be necessary for MTSD, such as conversations about the severity of the problem of dating violence, since both the mothers and teens would have experienced the problem first-hand. They also added more activities and emphasis to some areas they thought would be really important for this population.</p>
<p>Then, they did pilot testing with some families in the target population. First, they tried to go to local courts to recruit mothers who were getting protective orders, but they weren’t able to recruit many families. They used this contextual evidence to change their approach, and started recruiting families in different ways. Similarly, they started out doing focus groups with participants to get feedback on MTSD, but most of the families didn’t show up. So they cut out the need for participants to travel to a central location by doing phone interviews instead.</p>
<p>In these interviews, they asked participants all about their experiences with MTSD. They asked both mothers and teens what they thought about the program’s structure and content – what was interesting and what wasn’t, what was difficult to talk about, whether it was convenient, what they wished there were more of, and on and on. And they learned that though some of their logical and theory-based ideas for what to change were right, some of the elements that they took out were really still important for these mothers and teens. Participants also told the researchers that some of the things they emphasized should have been emphasized even more, which both illustrated the researchers’ understanding of the population and highlighted clear ways to make the program even more relevant. The researchers used all of this information to change MTSD before doing a full-scale study on the program.</p>
<p>What I love about this article is that the researchers didn’t just tell us how effective the new MTSD program is. They tell us how they got it to be right for the target population. We can use lessons from their process in our communities. Based on our understanding of our communities, we can make some informed guesses about what parts of evidence-based programs and risk and protective factors are and aren’t important for the community, and then, of course, actually check with the community. Even better, we can ask some members of the community what parts of a program they think are relevant to them, and then verify the changes we make with more members of the community. And when we find that something isn’t working for our community, like the court-based recruitment and focus groups in this study, we can change it.</p>
<p>It’s all about taking what works in research-based programs and making it work for us.</p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation</strong>: Foshee, V.A., Dixon, K.S., Ennett, S.T., Moracco, K.E., Bowling, J.M., Chang, L.Y., &amp; Moss, J.L. (2014). The process of adapting a universal dating abuse prevention program to adolescents exposed to domestic violence. <em>Journal of Interpersonal Violence. </em>Advance online publication.</p>
<p><strong>Link</strong>: http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/10/01/0886260514552278.abstract</p>
<p><strong>Full abstract</strong>:</p>
<blockquote><p>&#8220;Adolescents exposed to domestic violence are at increased risk of dating abuse, yet no evaluated dating abuse prevention programs have been designed specifically for this high-risk population. This article describes the process of adapting <em>Families for Safe Dates</em> (FSD), an evidenced-based universal dating abuse prevention program, to this high-risk population, including conducting 12 focus groups and 107 interviews with the target audience. FSD includes six booklets of dating abuse prevention information, and activities for parents and adolescents to do together at home. We adapted FSD for mothers who were victims of domestic violence, but who no longer lived with the abuser, to do with their adolescents who had been exposed to the violence. Through the adaptation process, we learned that families liked the program structure and valued being offered the program and that some of our initial assumptions about this population were incorrect. We identified practices and beliefs of mother victims and attributes of these adolescents that might increase their risk of dating abuse that we had not previously considered. In addition, we learned that some of the content of the original program generated negative family interactions for some. The findings demonstrate the utility of using a careful process to adapt evidence-based interventions (EBIs) to cultural sub-groups, particularly the importance of obtaining feedback on the program from the target audience. Others can follow this process to adapt EBIs to groups other than the ones for which the original EBI was designed.&#8221;</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Online sexual violence prevention program may show promise</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/online-sexual-violence-prevention-program-may-show-promise/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/online-sexual-violence-prevention-program-may-show-promise/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 14 Oct 2014 17:56:16 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bystander intervention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[colleges]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[online program]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[sexual violence prevention]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19050</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[There are a lot of online programs that colleges use to try to prevent sexual violence, especially during student orientation.&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>There are a lot of online programs that colleges use to try to prevent sexual violence, especially during student orientation. Many of these programs are only one session or only cover topics like legal definitions of sexual assault and knowledge about consent. A new web-based program evaluated at Georgia State University covered these topics, but it also incorporated information about social norms, bystander intervention, and more into six 30-minute sessions. The program used interactive activities and a serial drama that modeled desirable behaviors. It was theory-driven and addressed risk and protective factors. It was also rigorously evaluated, using a true experimental design, with random selection and assignment to the program or to a control group, which consisted of a web-based program on more generic health topics. Although the participants were limited to men at a large, urban, southern university, they were diverse in terms of race, grade point average, and other factors.</p>
<figure id="attachment_19051" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19051" style="width: 300px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/doctabu/291216582"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19051 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/laptop-300x195.png?x47887" alt="Image source: Flickr Creative Commons, Brian Moore" width="300" height="195" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/laptop-300x195.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/laptop.png 900w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19051" class="wp-caption-text">Image source: Flickr Creative Commons, Brian Moore</figcaption></figure>
<p>The web-based program, called RealConsent, showed some promise. In a follow-up survey six months after the study, men who had completed RealConsent reported better knowledge and attitudes than people in the control group on factors believed to lead to bystander intervention and to sexual assault perpetration. They showed better legal knowledge of sexual assault and rape, knowledge of effective consent, and intentions to intervene against rape-supportive attitudes and speech and sexually coercive behaviors; as well as less hostility toward women, acceptance of peers’ sexually coercive behavior, and hyper-gender ideology.</p>
<p>The study also measured actual behaviors, not just risk and protective factors. The RealConsent group was significantly more likely to report intervening against rape-supportive attitudes, speech, and behaviors, and significantly less likely to report perpetrating sexual assault than the control group.</p>
<p>There were some limitations to the study, though. Out of the 743 men who started the study and completed the program, only 215 made it all the way through the six-month follow-up survey. Similar numbers of participants dropped out of each group, and the researchers say that the people who dropped out of both groups were similar. Still, when so few participants made it the whole way through the study, it’s impossible to know if the people who did complete the study were different in some way that affected the results. It’s also hard to rely on self-report. It could be that after the RealConsent participants learned about legal definitions of rape and sexual assault, as well as about getting effective consent, they didn’t report any sexual coercion they perpetrated because they knew it was unacceptable.</p>
<p>Hopefully, there will be more evaluation of RealConsent and web-based programs like it in the future, so we can see if this program is good for students at other colleges and beyond. If more studies show that this multi-session, multi-topic web-based model is effective, it could be a great tool to reach large audiences with fewer resources.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation: </strong>Salazar, L.F., Vivolo-Kantor, A., Hardin, J., &amp; Berkowitz, A. (2014). <em>Journal of Medical Internet Research, 16</em>(9). doi:10.2196/jmir.3426</p>
<p><strong>Link to full article (free)</strong>: <a href="http://www.jmir.org/2014/9/e203/">http://www.jmir.org/2014/9/e203/</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract:</strong></p>
<blockquote><p><strong>Background: </strong>Bystander intervention approaches offer promise for reducing rates of sexual violence on college campuses. Most interventions are in-person small-group formats, which limit their reach and reduce their overall public health impact.<br />
<strong>Objective: </strong>This study evaluated the efficacy of RealConsent, a Web-based bystander approach to sexual violence prevention, in enhancing prosocial intervening behaviors and preventing sexual violence perpetration.<br />
<strong>Methods: </strong>A random probability sample of 743 male undergraduate students (aged 18 to 24 years) attending a large, urban university located in the southeastern United States was recruited online and randomized to either RealConsent (n=376) or a Web-based general health promotion program (n=367). Participants were surveyed online at baseline, postintervention, and 6-months postintervention. RealConsent was delivered via a password-protected Web portal that contained six 30-minute media-based and interactive modules covering knowledge of informed consent, communication skills regarding sex, the role of alcohol and male socialization in sexual violence, empathy for rape victims, and bystander education. Primary outcomes were self-reported prosocial intervening behaviors and sexual violence perpetration. Secondary outcomes were theoretical mediators (eg, knowledge, attitudes).<br />
<strong>Results: </strong>At 6-month follow-up RealConsent participants intervened more often (<em>P</em>=.04) and engaged in less sexual violence perpetration (<em>P</em>=.04) compared to controls. In addition, RealConsent participants reported greater legal knowledge of sexual assault (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), greater knowledge of effective consent (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), less rape myths (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), greater empathy for rape victims (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), less negative date rape attitudes (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), less hostility toward women (<em>P</em>=.01), greater intentions to intervene (<em>P</em>=.04), less hyper-gender ideology (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), less positive outcome expectancies for nonconsensual sex (<em>P</em>=.03), more positive outcome expectancies for intervening (<em>P</em>&lt;.001), and less comfort with other men’s inappropriate behaviors (<em>P</em>&lt;.001).<br />
<strong>Conclusions: </strong>Our results support the efficacy of RealConsent. Due to its Web-based format, RealConsent has potential for broad-based dissemination thereby increasing its overall public health impact on sexual violence.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Domestic and sexual violence rates much higher among people with severe mental illness</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/domestic-and-sexual-violence-rates-much-higher-among-people-with-severe-mental-illness/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/10/domestic-and-sexual-violence-rates-much-higher-among-people-with-severe-mental-illness/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 03 Oct 2014 16:46:25 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[domestic violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[intimate partner violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[mental illness]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[risk factors]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[sexual violence]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=19018</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A 2014 study from the United Kingdom shows stark differences in the occurrence and consequences of domestic and sexual violence victimization&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>A 2014 study from the United Kingdom shows stark differences in the occurrence and consequences of domestic and sexual violence victimization in the adult lives of people with severe mental illness (SMI), compared to the general population. Severe mental illness was defined in this study as having mental disorders that qualify them for having designated caseworkers from state health services. The researchers were able to survey a random sample of almost 300 people with SMI for the study, and the comparison with the general population came from a nationally representative sample of over 22,000 people.</p>
<p>You can see in the chart below the differences between rates for people with SMI (patients) and for the general population (controls). The study looked at how many people had experienced domestic violence and sexual assault since they were 16 years old, as well as in the past year alone.</p>
<figure id="attachment_19020" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-19020" style="width: 456px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-19020" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/SMI-chart.png?x47887" alt="Image source: Khalifeh et al., 2014" width="456" height="243" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/SMI-chart.png 552w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/10/SMI-chart-300x159.png 300w" sizes="(max-width: 456px) 100vw, 456px" /><figcaption id="caption-attachment-19020" class="wp-caption-text">Image source: Khalifeh et al., 2014</figcaption></figure>
<p>Among people who had experienced severe sexual assault (SSA), which the study defined as nonconsensual penetration or oral sex, people with SMI were also more likely to experience many kinds of negative effects than the general population. People with SMI were significantly more likely to have mental or emotional problems, difficulty sleeping, depression, mistrust of others, stopping going out, and attempting suicide. They were also more likely to have physical injuries from bruises to broken bones, pregnancy, and sexually transmitted infections, but this difference was not statistically significant.</p>
<p>Just to give you a clearer picture of what these consequences looked like, <strong>91.8%</strong> of people with SMI had adverse psychological consequences after SSA, as compared to 64.2% of the general population. And <strong>53.1%</strong> of these people with SMI attempted suicide as a result of SSA, as opposed to 3.4% of the general population.</p>
<p>What does this mean for prevention? Though this information came from Britain, it is probably safe to assume that there are similar trends in the United States. People with SMI appear to be extremely vulnerable to sexual and domestic violence, and they also have higher rates of severe health problems after SSA. First of all, we need more research about the special risk and protective factors for this population, so we know more about how to prevent sexual and domestic violence against them. Once we know more, some primary prevention efforts should clearly be targeted toward people who live with, care for, and regularly interact with people with SMI.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation: </strong>Khalifeh, K. et al. (2014). Domestic and sexual violence against patients with severe mental illness. <em>Psychological Medicine</em>. Advance online publication. doi:10.1017/S0033291714001962</p>
<p><strong>Link to full article (free): </strong><a href="http://journals.cambridge.org/download.php?file=%2FPSM%2FS0033291714001962a.pdf&amp;code=a1d706d3812b8bb7e6bbfbf916a408e1">http://journals.cambridge.org/download.php?file=%2FPSM%2FS0033291714001962a.pdf&amp;code=a1d706d3812b8bb7e6bbfbf916a408e1</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract:</strong></p>
<blockquote><p><strong>Background</strong>. Domestic and sexual violence are significant public health problems but little is known about the extent to which men and women with severe mental illness (SMI) are at risk compared with the general population. We aimed to compare the prevalence and impact of violence against SMI patients and the general population.</p>
<p><strong>Method</strong>. Three hundred and three randomly recruited psychiatric patients, in contact with community services for 51 year, were interviewed using the British Crime Survey domestic/sexual violence questionnaire. Prevalence and correlates of violence in this sample were compared with those from 22606 general population controls participating in the contemporaneous 2011/12 national crime survey.</p>
<p><strong>Results</strong>. Past-year domestic violence was reported by 27% v. 9% of SMI and control women, respectively [odds ratio (OR) adjusted for socio-demographics, aOR 2.7, 95% confidence interval (CI) 1.7–4.0], and by 13% v. 5% of SMI and control men, respectively (aOR 1.6, 95% CI 1.0–2.8). Past-year sexual violence was reported by 10% v. 2.0% of SMI and control women respectively (aOR 2.9, 95% CI 1.4–5.8). Family (non-partner) violence comprised a greater proportion of overall domestic violence among SMI than control victims (63% v. 35%, p&lt;0.01). Adulthood serious sexual assault led to attempted suicide more often among SMI than control female victims (53% v. 3.4%, p&lt;0.001).</p>
<p><strong>Conclusions</strong>. Compared to the general population, patients with SMI are at substantially increased risk of domestic and sexual violence, with a relative excess of family violence and adverse health impact following victimization. Psychiatric services, and public health and criminal justice policies, need to address domestic and sexual violence in this at-risk group.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>NSVRC releases report on key findings about sexual violence prevention strategies</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/nsvrc-releases-report-on-key-findings-about-sexual-violence-prevention-strategies/</link>

		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 24 Sep 2014 18:05:40 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[NSVRC]]></category>

		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/nsvrc-releases-report-on-key-findings-about-sexual-violence-prevention-strategies/</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[The National Sexual Violence Research Center (NSVRC) has released a user-friendly practitioner&#8217;s guide to a 2014 research article by Sarah&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft wp-image-19001 size-full" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/image001-1.jpg?x47887" alt="image001 (1)" width="200" height="259" />The National Sexual Violence Research Center (NSVRC) has released a user-friendly practitioner&#8217;s guide to a 2014 research article by Sarah DeGue et al. The original research article was a systematic review of the effectiveness of sexual violence primary prevention programs and strategies. According to DeGue et al.&#8217;s study, the only programs/strategies that have been demonstrated to be effective are Safe Dates, Shifting Boundaries, and the Violence Against Women Act of 1994. The article also identified some strategies that might be effective, even though evaluations haven&#8217;t been consistent enough to declare them definitely effective. Finally, the study found some programs to have no effect or even be potentially harmful.</p>
<p>Here&#8217;s a link to the NSVRC&#8217;s report, <a href="http://www.nsvrc.org/sites/default/files/publications_nsvrc_guide_key-findings-systematic-review-primary-prevention-strategies.pdf">Key Findings from A Systematic Review of Primary Prevention Strategies for Sexual Violence Perpetration</a>.</p>
<p>The research article that the NSVRC&#8217;s report is based on is can be found in full at no cost <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1359178914000536">here</a>.</p>
<p><strong>Full citation:</strong> DeGue, S., Valle, L. A., Holt, M. K., Massetti, G. M., Matjasko, J. L., &amp; Tharp, A. T. (2014). A systematic review of primary prevention strategies for sexual violence perpetration. <em>Aggression and Violent Behavior, 19</em>(4), 346-362. doi: 10.1016/j.avb.2014.05.004</p>

		<item>
		<title>School Staff’s Role in Preventing Bullying and Sexual Harassment</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/school-staffs-role-in-preventing-bullying-and-sexual-harassment/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/school-staffs-role-in-preventing-bullying-and-sexual-harassment/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 16 Sep 2014 17:15:11 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bullying]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bystander intervention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[school]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[school climate]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Sexual Harassment]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18982</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A big area of interest in research over the past few years has been how school climate, and particularly school&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_18983" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-18983" style="width: 300px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/chicago2016/3277193678"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-18983 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/3277193678_464166c8ab_z-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="Image source: Chicago 2016 on Flickr Creative Commons https://www.flickr.com/photos/chicago2016/3277193678" width="300" height="200" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/3277193678_464166c8ab_z-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/3277193678_464166c8ab_z.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-18983" class="wp-caption-text">Image source: Chicago 2016 on Flickr Creative Commons https://www.flickr.com/photos/chicago2016/3277193678</figcaption></figure>
<p>A big area of interest in research over the past few years has been how school climate, and particularly school staff, can influence bullying and sexual harassment. A recent study by Dorothy Espelage, Joshua Polanin, and Sabina Low adds to this body of research by looking at the connections between student reports related to bullying and teachers’, staff’s, and administrators’ perceptions. The study used a wide variety of questions to measure students’ and adults’ perceptions of middle schools’ climates, including likelihood of student intervention, likelihood of staff intervention, perceptions of aggression as a problem at their school, the school’s commitment to bullying prevention, positive teacher-staff-student interactions, and gender equity or intolerance of sexual harassment.</p>
<p>Overall, the study found that adults’ perceptions corresponded well to students’ perceptions. For example, when staff was perceived as likely to intervene to help victimized youth, students reported greater willingness to intervene in similar situations themselves. This is a great example of how the school climate and community can create norms to help prevent bullying, and specifically how important adults’ role is in building these norms. Teachers and administrators who take action to support prevention can have a big impact.</p>
<p>Importantly, the study also showed an association between staff reporting higher levels of gender equity and intolerance of sexual harassment, and students reporting lower bullying, lower bullying victimization, lower aggression, and higher willingness for students to intervene. It’s not really clear how the relationship between bullying, gender equity, and sexual harassment works, but this is yet another study showing that there is some sort of link. Reducing bullying seems to be an important way to affect sexual violence risk factors and sexual harassment, especially during early adolescence.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation: </strong>Espelage, D. L., Polanin, J. R., &amp; Low, S. K. (2014). Teacher and staff perceptions of school environment as predictors of student aggression, victimization, and willingness to intervene in bullying situations. <em>School Psychology Quarterly</em>. Advance online publication. doi: 10.1037/spq0000072</p>
<p><strong>Link: </strong>http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/25089334<strong> </strong></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract: </strong>This study examines how teacher and staff perceptions of the school environment correlate with student self-reports of bullying, aggression, victimization, and willingness to intervene in bullying incidents using multi-informant, multilevel modeling. Data were derived from 3,616 6th grade students across 36 middle schools in the Midwest, who completed survey measures of bullying, aggression, victimization, and willingness to intervene in bullying situations. Teachers and staff (n = 1,447) completed a school environment survey. Bivariate associations between school-level and student self-reports indicated that as teacher and staff perceive aggression as a problem in their school, students reported greater bully perpetration, fighting, peer victimization, and less willingness to intervene. Further, as staff and teacher report greater commitment to prevent bullying and viewed positive teacher and student relationships, there was less bullying, fighting, and peer victimization, and greater willingness to intervene. In a model where all school environment scales were entered together, a school commitment to prevent bullying was associated with less bullying, fighting, and peer victimization. Student-reports of bully perpetration and peer victimization were largely explained by staff and teacher commitment to bully prevention, whereas fighting and willingness to intervene were largely explained by student characteristics (e.g., gender). We conclude that efforts to address bullying and victimization should involve support from the school administration. School psychologists should play an active role in the school climate improvement process, by creating a school climate council consisting of students, parents, and teachers; administering school climate measures; identifying specific school improvement targets from these data, and engaging all stakeholders in the ongoing school improvement plan.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>2011 National Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence Survey (NISVS) findings released</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/2011-national-intimate-partner-and-sexual-violence-survey-nisvs-findings-released/</link>

		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>

					<description><![CDATA[Today, the CDC released a new data set for the Prevalence and Characteristics of Sexual Violence, Stalking, and Intimate Partner&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/images/nisvs-infographic-web.jpg" class="broken_link"><img loading="lazy" class="alignright wp-image-18960 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/short-280x300.png?x47887" alt="short" width="280" height="300" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/short-280x300.png 280w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/short.png 790w" sizes="(max-width: 280px) 100vw, 280px" /></a>Today, the CDC released a new data set for the <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/ss6308a1.htm?s_cid=ss6308a1_e" target="_blank"><em>Prevalence and Characteristics of Sexual Violence, Stalking, and Intimate Partner Violence Victimization – National Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence Survey</em></a> for 2011.</p>
<p>The new report contains similar findings to the previous NISVS data sets, which suggests that the survey’s measures are reliable.This information highlights the prevalence of sexual violence and is critical as we continue our sexual violence prevention efforts.</p>
<p>A few key findings from the report, which you will find are consistent with the 2010 NISVS data, include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Nearly 1 in 5 women (19%) and 1 in 59 men (nearly 2%) in the U.S. have been raped at some time in their lives.</li>
<li>One in 5 women (22%) and 1 in 7 men (14%) reported experiencing severe physical violence by an intimate partner in their lifetime.</li>
<li>One in 7 women (15%) and 1 in 18 men (6%) have experienced stalking victimization during their lifetime in which they felt very fearful or believed that they or someone close to them would be harmed or killed. Much of stalking victimization was facilitated by technology (i.e., unwanted phone calls and text messages).</li>
<li>Among women who have been raped and men who have been made to penetrate a perpetrator, 4 in 10 women (40%) and 1 in 5 men (21%) first experienced that type of victimization before age 18, and 4 in 5 women (79%) and 7 in 10 men (71%) were first victimized before age 25.</li>
</ul>
<p>Findings from this report can be used to help demonstrate that sexual violence, intimate partner violence, and stalking create a considerable public health burden, and can help identify priority target populations for prevention. Specifically, the data confirms the importance of efforts that focus on preventing these types of violence against young people.</p>
<p>In March 2014, PreventConnect hosted a series of web conferences to review this study in greater detail. Check out recordings of these PreventConnect web conferences about NISVS where you can hear from the researchers and advocates discussing the implications of the findings of previous versions of the report:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/03/webconf_nisvs/">Findings from the National Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence Survey: Implications for Prevention</a></li>
<li><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/03/nisvs_lgbt_findings_/">Findings on Victimization by Sexual Orientation: Implications for Policy</a></li>
<li><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/03/webconf_nisvs/">Findings on Victimization by Sexual Orientation: Implications for Preventing Sexual Violence and IPV in LBGT Communities</a></li>
</ul>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

		<item>
		<title>What does your neighborhood have to do with sexual violence?</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/what-does-your-neighborhood-have-to-do-with-sexual-violence/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/what-does-your-neighborhood-have-to-do-with-sexual-violence/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 03 Sep 2014 18:05:10 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Community]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Moving to Opportunity]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[neighborhood]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[protective factors]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[risk factors]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[sexual violence prevention]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18953</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[I’ve been wanting to write a post about the impact of neighborhood conditions on sexual violence for a while now,&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_18954" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-18954" style="width: 300px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/neighborhood.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-18954 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/neighborhood-300x199.jpg?x47887" alt="Image source: North Charleston, Flickr Creative Commons, https://www.flickr.com/photos/northcharleston/" width="300" height="199" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/neighborhood-300x199.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/neighborhood.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-18954" class="wp-caption-text">Image source: North Charleston, Flickr Creative Commons, https://www.flickr.com/photos/northcharleston/</figcaption></figure>
<p>I’ve been wanting to write a post about the impact of neighborhood conditions on sexual violence for a while now, and it&#8217;s going to be a long one.</p>
<p>As a public health person, I’ve been reading about how important neighborhoods are to health for years. <a href="http://www.rwjf.org/content/dam/farm/reports/issue_briefs/2011/rwjf70450" target="_blank">Neighborhood factors</a> can make residents more or less likely to suffer a <a href="http://www.colorado.edu/ibs/pop/boardman/articles/2004_Boardman_SS%26M.pdf" target="_blank">range of health problems</a>, from higher rates of chronic disease and obesity-related disease, to poorer mental health, to increased rates of <a href="http://hsb.sagepub.com/content/49/2/131.short" target="_blank" class="broken_link">asthma</a>, to engagement in <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0277953614003967" target="_blank">risky health behaviors</a>. <a href="http://www.sciencemag.org/content/277/5328/918.short" target="_blank">Neighborhoods</a> have also been shown to have important <a href="http://ccj.sagepub.com/content/20/2/127.short" target="_blank" class="broken_link">effects on many types of violence</a>. <a href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1741-3737.2002.00833.x/abstract;jsessionid=C252C60E725E9AE85E08DF10E0846CB9.f01t04?deniedAccessCustomisedMessage=&amp;userIsAuthenticated=false" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Studies</a> have <a href="http://www.annalsofepidemiology.org/article/S1047-2797(00)00052-1/abstract" target="_blank">shown</a> an association between intimate partner violence and child maltreatment and neighborhood-level factors, like high unemployment, social disadvantage, residents’ perceived level of <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19297887" target="_blank">neighborhood problems</a>, and neighbors’ <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/intimatepartnerviolence/riskprotectivefactors.html" target="_blank">willingness</a> to intervene in violence. They have also <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0145213414002555" target="_blank">shown</a> that programs that seek to improve neighborhood conditions can prevent some of these kinds of violence.</p>
<p>We also know that sexual violence <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/ncipc/pub-res/research_agenda/07_violence.htm" target="_blank" class="broken_link">shares</a> several risk and protective <a href="http://www.purdue.edu/incsapp/resources/organizations/VirginiaGuidelines.pdf" target="_blank" class="broken_link">factors</a> with other kinds of violence, such as rigid gender roles, the “isms,” strength of community sanctions for violence, absence or presence of healthy relationship role models, etc. So it seems possible that neighborhood factors could impact rates of sexual violence. There has not been very much research about this question, but a <a href="http://www.urban.org/uploadedpdf/411974_place_matters.pdf" target="_blank">report</a> from the Urban Institute has some interesting insights about how neighborhood settings and sexual violence might be related. The report talks about the decade-long <a href="http://portal.hud.gov/hudportal/HUD?src=/programdescription/mto" target="_blank">Moving to Opportunity</a> study, in which some families living in public housing in high-poverty, “chronically disadvantaged” neighborhoods were supported to move to affordable housing in low-poverty neighborhoods. It’s important to note that nothing about the families’ socioeconomic statuses changed because of the study; families just moved from one neighborhood to another.</p>
<p>The studies were looking for changes in mental health and risky behaviors in adolescents, and found “dramatic improvements” for adolescent girls, but not for boys. Based on interviews with these girls, one potential explanation was that for girls, moving to a low-poverty neighborhood meant “a profound reduction in fear of sexual harassment, coercion, and violence.” They “theorize that certain high poverty neighborhoods are characterized by a coercive sexual environment,” where adolescent girls (and probably women more generally) are exposed to high risk of sexual harassment and coercion.</p>
<p>But what about all the girls and women who were not able to move away from the high-poverty, disadvantaged neighborhoods? Can the sexually coercive environment they live in be changed? Can programs at a neighborhood level reduce rates of sexual harassment and sexual violence? There’s so much more we’d need to know about which neighborhood factors create these sexually coercive environments, and how those factors can be changed. Perhaps things like <a href="http://eab.sagepub.com/content/22/1/27.abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">neighborhood cohesion</a> (strong social networks, a sense of community, neighbors helping each other), <a href="http://www.uky.edu/~eushe2/Pajares/effbook11.html" target="_blank">collective efficacy</a> (belief in the group’s ability to organize and make changes), or residents’ perception of neighborhood safety could be important here. A subsequent <a href="http://uar.sagepub.com.libproxy.lib.unc.edu/content/45/6/715" target="_blank">article</a> on this study from 2013 did explore the girls’ and their parents’ experience of trying to stay safe in sexually coercive environments, but did not really focus on these questions.</p>
<p>What does your experiential evidence as a prevention practitioner tell you? How do you think neighborhood sexually coercive environments are created? Do you think that neighborhood-level risk and protective factors, like neighborhood cohesion, stability, collective efficacy, poverty, and general levels of violence, have something to do with it? Do you think there are any changes that could be made in a neighborhood that would help prevent at least some kinds of sexual violence? Is sexual violence too pervasive and widespread to address at a neighborhood level, or could this be an important piece of the puzzle?</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full citation:</strong> Popkin, S. J., Acs, G., &amp; Smith, R. E. (2009). <em>The Urban Institute’s program on neighborhoods and youth development: Understanding how place matters for kids</em>. Washington, DC: The Urban Institute.</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://www.urban.org/publications/411974.html">http://www.urban.org/publications/411974.html</a></p>
<p><strong>Full abstract:</strong> A central goal of U.S. social welfare policy is to ensure that all children have the opportunity to reach their full potential as productive adults. Yet it is increasingly clear that where children live plays a central role in determining their life chances. This paper provides an overview of The Urban Institute&#8217;s Program on Neighborhoods and Youth Development, which is dedicated to understanding the relationships between neighborhood-level factors and the well-being and development of children and youth and identifying and evaluating place-based, community-wide strategies to help children grow up to reach their full potential as adults.</p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/what-does-your-neighborhood-have-to-do-with-sexual-violence/feed/</wfw:commentRss>

		<item>
		<title>Forcing Gender Roles, Harming Health</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/forcing-gender-roles-harming-health/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/forcing-gender-roles-harming-health/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 02 Sep 2014 21:54:09 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[gender]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[gender roles]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[norms]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18949</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A new study out of Portugal confirms what many sexual and relationship health promoters, various curricula, and other U.S. and&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/Screen-Shot-2014-09-02-at-2.47.49-PM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-18950" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/Screen-Shot-2014-09-02-at-2.47.49-PM.png?x47887" alt="blue background with off white male and female stick figures side by side" width="274" height="285" /></a>A new study out of Portugal confirms what many sexual and relationship health promoters, various curricula, and other <a href="http://www.gb.nrao.edu/~sheather/new%20lit/building%20self%20efficacy_identity/whatkindofgirl.pdf">U.S.</a> and <a href="http://psycnet.apa.org/psycinfo/1995-98749-000">British</a> studies have asserted: forcing young people to adhere to gender norms hurts their health.</p>
<p>In an observational study of 14 year olds, Maria do Mar Pereira, deputy director of the University of Warwick’s <a href="http://www2.warwick.ac.uk/fac/soc/sociology/rsw/research_centres/gender/" class="broken_link">Centre for the Study of Women and Gender</a>, observed girls restricting their behavior so that they could appear “more feminine” (i.e. avoiding physical activity and putting themselves on diets) and boys facing pressure to behave in ways that proved “their manliness” (i.e. fighting, drinking unhealthy amounts of alcohol, suppressing emotions).  The study concludes that:</p>
<blockquote><p>…this constant effort to manage one’s everyday life in line with gender norms produces significant anxiety, insecurity, stress and low self-esteem for both boys and girls, and both for ‘popular’ young people and those who have lower status in school.</p></blockquote>
<p>And, surprise, surprise – the teens studied <em>did not like</em> that they were expected to follow these gender roles. In an article about the study, Pereira recalls sharing the results of the study with the teens. They were shocked! Learning that their peers felt the same way and were also performing based on gendered expectations, their <em>behaviors changed</em>. They stopped mocking each other for not filling the expected gender roles, there was less fighting, and more.</p>
<p>In addition to the obvious link between gendered norms and sexual and domestic violence, this study also suggests something positive – we can do something to change harmful norms around gender. As Pereira pointed out in an interview about the study:</p>
<blockquote><p>Sometimes adults think it’s impossible to change gender norms because they’re already so deeply entrenched. But they’re much more entrenched in adults than they are in young people&#8230;It’s actually fairly easy to reach young people if you create opportunities for discussion, if you get them to think about their own experiences.</p></blockquote>
<p>Read another article about the study <a href="http://www2.warwick.ac.uk/newsandevents/pressreleases/girls_feel_they/">here</a>.</p>
<p>Read more about the study in the resultant book <a href="https://www.imprensa.ics.ul.pt/index.php?main_page=product_book_info&amp;cPath=5&amp;products_id=318&amp;zenid=tbfo10nu0go7p4n0of25ku7bs0">A negociação do Género em espaço escolar</a> (<em>Doing Gender in the Playground: The Negotiation of Gender and Sexuality in Schools</em>)<em>.</em></p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/09/forcing-gender-roles-harming-health/feed/</wfw:commentRss>
			<slash:comments>0</slash:comments>
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">18949</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>Train the teachers?</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/08/train-the-teachers/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/08/train-the-teachers/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 13 Aug 2014 18:03:42 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[high school]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[middle school]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[school programs]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[teacher training]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[train the trainer]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18915</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A new study has brought up interesting questions for me about the tried and true strategy of prevention education for&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_18916" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-18916" style="width: 320px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/wwworks/6107910264/in/photolist-dm3QUf-7wD9zk-cCJFe9-aiJCVE-dm3RNm-dm3LE6-dm3QVL-dm3LBn-dienSP-dtonc9-dmYTui-dtozc7-mckoua-dQwH6a-dm3LAP-dm3LAi-dm3LF6-dQxiSR-dQCUkf-4GmjT5-mhRAeb-6u1cJ6-6pyfYp-tBV5d-a9Ujs8-jVA2F9-jVzK5d-jVAmLd-jVyC4F-7Ck8wt-azj5bZ-gV2wyk-5HrNGA-tBUhu-6ZA2hd-jVy5g2-jVxFCP-jVzTPs-jVxU9a-jVxdJt-jVAoib-jVy39g-9b53Ew-jVAjej-jVA1Ts-jVyAxp-jVAknm-jVxrmc-jVyus8-jVxXD4/"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-18916" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/6107910264_7755c3029f_z-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="" width="320" height="213" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/6107910264_7755c3029f_z-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/6107910264_7755c3029f_z.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-18916" class="wp-caption-text">Image from woodleywonderworks</figcaption></figure>
<p>A <a href="http://psycnet.apa.org/journals/aap/5/2/106/" target="_blank">new study</a> has brought up interesting questions for me about the tried and true strategy of prevention education for youth. Prevention education staff at the Sexual Abuse Treatment Center in Hawai’i created a curriculum called <em>Respect</em>, which used materials, content, and language relevant to the schools’ diverse student populations. The curriculum successfully improved student knowledge, attitudes, and self-efficacy to intervene as bystanders. What was really distinctive about this program, though, was that instead of delivering a prevention curriculum directly to students, the prevention education staff trained school staff to teach the curriculum themselves.</p>
<p>According to the study, this approach could help the curriculum to become a standard part of health education classes. This would have the added benefit that, “given the struggles that many communities face in funding prevention efforts, it [is] advantageous to create a curriculum that could be taught by a variety of individuals within existing institutions, not simply by outside experts in the field.” The study also mentions that having teachers and counselors deliver the curricula can leverage the existing relationships between school staff and students to improve students’ acceptance of the ideas in the curriculum.</p>
<p>While the article started to explain why this train-the-trainer approach could be beneficial, they didn’t evaluate whether or how having school staff teach the curriculum affected the success of the program. I think these could really be the key questions about this approach, and I think training the trainer could be important for a lot of reasons. Theoretically, having school staff teach could increase their understanding of and buy-in to prevention messages. In this study, only one teacher was trained to deliver the curriculum. But what if multiple counselors and teachers at every grade level completed a training and delivered the curriculum? Would they be more invested in preventing sexual violence? Would teachers and counselors become more involved as active bystanders themselves? Would they shape the school climate to be less tolerant of or indifferent to sexual harassment and assault? Could they even change the school environment and social norms? For me, the answer is an enthusiastic maybe!</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full Citation: </strong>Baker, C. K., Naai, R., Mitchell, J., &amp; Trecker, C. (2014). Utilizing a train-the-trainer model for sexual violence prevention: Findings from a pilot study with high school students of Asian and Pacific Islander descent in Hawai’i. <em>Asian American Journal of Psychology, 5</em>(2), 106-115. doi: 10.1037/a0034670</p>
<p><strong>Link: </strong><a href="http://psycnet.apa.org/journals/aap/5/2/106/" target="_blank">http://psycnet.apa.org/journals/aap/5/2/106/</a></p>
<p><strong>Full Abstract:</strong> Sexual violence is a significant public health problem with potential long-term consequences for victims. Prior research suggests that adolescents are at increased risk for sexual violence, which makes prevention education programs critical for this age group. However, there are few prevention programs that specifically target sexual violence, and even fewer that are culturally appropriate for Hawai‘i’s diverse population. This pilot study addresses these two gaps by implementing and evaluating a culturally grounded school-based sexual violence prevention curriculum. The <em>Respect</em> curriculum was developed by the Sex Abuse Treatment Center (a statewide social service agency with expertise in sexual violence prevention and treatment), in collaboration with the Hawai‘i Department of Education, teachers, counselors, and students across the state. A train-the-trainer model was used for implementation, in which teachers were trained and supported in teaching the curriculum to their students. One high school on Oahu served as the intervention school, with a demographically similar high school serving as the comparison. The sample was comprised of 136 students: 63 in the intervention school and 73 in the comparison school. Results showed that students in the intervention school significantly increased their knowledge of sexual violence, decreased their victim-blaming attitudes, and increased their bystander self-efficacy (i.e., their likelihood of acting on behalf of a potential victim if a situation arose) compared with students in the comparison school. Findings provide preliminary support for the utility of a train-the-trainer model in addressing sensitive health topics.</p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/08/train-the-teachers/feed/</wfw:commentRss>

		<item>
		<title>Serious lack of research on preventing violence against people with disabilities</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/serious-lack-of-research-on-preventing-violence-against-people-with-disabilities/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/serious-lack-of-research-on-preventing-violence-against-people-with-disabilities/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 14 Jul 2014 16:58:59 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[child abuse prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[child sexual abuse prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[disabilities]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[violence prevention]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18871</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Have you ever tried to find good research or best practices for preventing violence against people with disabilities? Did you&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_18872" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-18872" style="width: 281px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/83633410@N07/7658298768/in/photolist-cEJMpW-6DTbAF-KLduT-9fwoMs-5UNWTt-a5iPKr-dF2kfy-45cqRN-2gemv-7o37h4-6w3EcN-fH9nR7-6znZE-weYd8-6rmstp-eoKTfj-h4yNV6-D2Gaj-5eGQeG-7kvGF1-c4Rw4G-2M9UdB-bTci2-6eBt8y-fGr7ae-4kQjs2-4CvS3s-pqxcb-7JgWR3-4HmEfn-7A8Nn4-3AG48-3PiCZ2-9bFkti-gjAsrq-3AG3n-3AG3J-8d2LFo-5Tvbpk-3j3pov-s9wkh-aLUUMe-dM5ip5-6dDSrE-KTPYW-dRaMFW-6xFWc6-63UskT-65MDht-8uh4rn/"><img loading="lazy" class="wp-image-18872" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/confused-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="" width="281" height="187" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/confused-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/confused.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 281px) 100vw, 281px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-18872" class="wp-caption-text">Source: CollegeDegrees360</figcaption></figure>
<p>Have you ever tried to find good research or best practices for preventing violence against people with disabilities? Did you end up frustrated and empty-handed? If so, you’re not alone. In a new research article, Christopher Mikton, Holly Maguire, and Tom Shakespeare did a systematic review of the research on violence prevention for people with disabilities, and they specifically wanted to look at studies that evaluated how effective <a href="http://wiki.preventconnect.org/Primary%2C+Secondary%2C+Tertiary+Prevention" target="_blank">primary and secondary prevention</a> efforts were for this population.</p>
<p>They found some practice guides, like <em><a href="http://kc.vanderbilt.edu/kennedy_files/ProtectingChildrenwithDisabilitiesfromSexualAssaultAParentsGuide.pdf" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Protecting Children with Disabilities from Sexual Assault: A Parent’s Guide</a> </em>and <a href="http://www.researchgate.net/publication/258224697_Childhood_Disability__Violence._Empowering_disability_organisations_to_develop_prevention_strategies" target="_blank"><em>Childhood, Disability &amp; Violence</em></a>, which both make recommendations for violence prevention based on practitioners’ experiences, not on research. While it is great that these guidelines are based in real practice, it would be nice if there were also a body of research-based evidence to support them.</p>
<p>Even though the researchers looked for studies that evaluated the effectiveness of <em>any</em> kind of prevention efforts for people with <em>any</em> kind of disability in <em>any</em> country, they only found 10 studies that fit their criteria. Based on pre-set standards, the article deemed that all 10 of these studies were weak in their research methods, and <em>none</em> of them found that the prevention strategies they studied were effective.</p>
<p>Though this study clearly shows that the research world is way behind on preventing violence against people with disabilities, hopefully it will serve as a wake-up call to researchers. <strong>If you’ve had some good violence prevention experiences with people with disabilities, it could be worth connecting with Mikton, Maguire, and Shakespeare.</strong> I’m sure this article will generate interest in new research on this topic, and you might be able to help inform some of that research.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full Citation:</strong> Mikton, C., Maguire, H., &amp; Shakespeare, T. (2014). A systematic review of the effectiveness of interventions to prevent and respond to violence against persons with disabilities. <em>Journal of Interpersonal Violence</em>. Epublished: May 28, 2014. Doi: 10.1177/0886260514534530</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/05/23/0886260514534530.refs" target="_blank" class="broken_link">http://jiv.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/05/23/0886260514534530.refs</a></p>
<p><strong>Full Abstract:</strong> Persons with disabilities make up some 15% of the world’s population and are at higher risk of violence. Yet there is currently no systematic review of the effectiveness of interventions to prevent violence against them. Thus the aim of this review was to systematically search for, appraise the quality of, and synthesize the evidence for the effectiveness of interventions to prevent and mitigate the consequences of all the main forms of interpersonal violence against people with all types of disabilities. The method used consisted of searches of eleven electronic databases, hand searches of three journals, scanning of reference lists of review articles, contact with experts, appraisal of risk of bias using the Quality Assessment Tool for Quantitative Studies, and narrative synthesis of results. This resulted in 736 titles being identified, 10 of which met the inclusion criteria and 6 and 2 addressed people with intellectual disabilities and developmental disabilities, respectively. Only one was from a low- and middle-income country. All studies received a weak rating on the quality assessment tool and none could be considered effective after taking risk of bias into account. In sum, the current evidence base offers little guidance to policy makers, program commissioners, and persons with disabilities for selecting interventions. More and higher quality research is required, particularly from low- and middle-income countries and on other forms of disability such as physical impairments, sensory impairments, and mental health conditions.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Culturally grounded prevention to create change</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/culturally-grounded-prevention-to-create-change/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/culturally-grounded-prevention-to-create-change/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 08 Jul 2014 20:01:08 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Community]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[culture]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18866</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Using approaches that are based in evidence is important, but it’s just as important to use approaches that make sense&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="https://www.flickr.com/photos/oregondot/4046764327/in/photolist-31RKR-bADzCf-gHr6bw-7aAJ3K-av1aCV-8JwWux-frimb8-cnnheh-gkyu3S-n8NAL-ekes5c-ekkhpS-ekkaNA-cB21rq-dBorZR-gJHDev-gJJRka-518WSd-fPjXUo-cB1ZQE-4uB8v8-5ahqSS-5MG6eg-8Xw8fQ-drryqs-9PhyYG-dNHCxJ-e9ToYW-ejTVyX-gJJeHb-4TFf8e-7R8CrE-7aAHov-7aAJNM-4fMfrq-4SEHQP-35hUrf-djpdQf-gHB5NY-gT5tqk-4QnASf-9pKoXp-8LVtVx-4SggnJ-8JExtY-9tbDkc-W3uYd-7oPLRf-cgiKnb-dyrDUg" target="_blank"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft wp-image-18867 size-medium" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/4046764327_396befce65_z-300x201.jpg?x47887" alt="From: Oregon Department of Transportation" width="300" height="201" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/4046764327_396befce65_z-300x201.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/4046764327_396befce65_z.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a>Using approaches that are based in evidence is important, but it’s just as important to use approaches that make sense in the culture you’re delivering them in. One of the major advantages a local agency can have in preventing sexual violence is knowing the communities they serve, so they can tailor their work to the community’s culture. There are lots of different ways to consider culture when you’re planning your prevention approaches. I found the 2014 article, &#8220;<a href="http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10935-013-0334-z">A continuum of approaches toward prevention interventions: From adaptation to grounding</a>&#8221; by Scott Okamoto and colleagues, and I think it does a great job of laying out the benefits and challenges of several methods of balancing evidence and culture.</p>
<p>The article talks about surface- and deep-structure cultural changes to existing prevention programs and strategies, which can range from translating materials from an evidence-based intervention into another language to working with community members to highlight core cultural values in a curriculum. These are the most common ways that people have considered culture in prevention so far, and they both make the evidence-based approaches more relevant and accessible to the communities they serve. But both of these approaches still might miss some key cultural issues that are preventing positive change.</p>
<p>The authors of this article have been working on developing an approach they call “culturally grounded prevention.” This approach involves using “methods that place the culture and social context of the targeted population at the center of the intervention” by developing the program from the “ground up” while still based on demonstrated prevention component. “Rather than adapting prevention curricular components from one population to another, they build the evidence base within the communities and cultures that are intended to be served.” This approach takes a lot of time and resources, but it might just create the most culturally appropriate prevention programs, especially when you’re working with a population that hasn’t been studied or evaluated enough in the past.</p>
<p>Different levels of cultural adaptation are useful in different situations. The article challenges us to think about how to balance evidence, fidelity, and resource constraints with cultural fit and specificity.</p>
<p>Do you think the community you serve could benefit from more culturally grounded prevention work?</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Full Citation:</strong> Okamoto, S. K., Kulis, S., Marsiglia, F. F., Holleran Steiker, L. K., &amp; Dustman, P. (2014). A continuum of approaches toward prevention interventions: From adaptation to grounding. <em>Journal of Primary Prevention, (35)</em>2, pp 103-12.</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10935-013-0334-z</p>
<p><strong>Full Abstract:</strong> The purpose of this article is to describe a conceptual model of methods used to develop culturally focused interventions. We describe a continuum of approaches ranging from non-adapted/surface-structure adapted programs to culturally grounded programs, and present recent examples of interventions resulting from the application of each of these approaches. The model has implications for categorizing culturally focused prevention efforts more accurately, and for gauging the time, resources, and level of community engagement necessary to develop programs using each of the different methods. The model also has implications for funding decisions related to the development and evaluation of programs, and for planning of participatory research approaches with community members.</p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/07/culturally-grounded-prevention-to-create-change/feed/</wfw:commentRss>

		<item>
		<title>Adverse Family Environment: A breeding ground for sexual offending</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/06/adverse-family-environment-a-breeding-ground-for-sexual-offending/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/06/adverse-family-environment-a-breeding-ground-for-sexual-offending/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Deena Fulton]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 09 Jun 2014 23:03:11 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[ACE Study]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[ACES]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[risk factors]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18815</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[In the sexual violence prevention field, one of the things we are always asking ourselves is why people commit sexually&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure style="width: 530px" class="wp-caption aligncenter"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/03/ACEs_Page_3.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/03/ACEs_Page_3.jpg?x47887" alt="" width="530" height="543" /></a><figcaption class="wp-caption-text">vetoviolence.cdc.gov</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the sexual violence prevention field, one of the things we are always asking ourselves is why people commit sexually violent acts. The answers are multiple and complicated, but a recent study by Levenson, Willis, and Prescott (2014) in Sex Abuse: A Journal of Research and Treatment reveals that male sex offenders in treatment programs are significantly more likely to report having a variety of adverse childhood experiences (<a title="Infographic: How adverse childhood experiences affect our lives and society" href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/03/infographic-how-adverse-childhod-experience-affect-our-lives-and-society/" target="_blank">ACEs</a>) than males in the general population.</p>
<p>The 679 male sex offenders in the study were more likely to have been verbally abused, physically abused, sexually abused, emotionally neglected, and physically neglected, as children than are males in the general population. They were also more likely to come from “dysfunctional homes,” in which their families dealt with domestic violence, substance abuse, mental illness, and incarceration. The more of these ACEs the sex offenders in the study reported having, the more likely they were to have victims who were younger than 12 and to have been convicted of sexual offenses that were violent, involved a weapon, and resulted in injuries for the victim.</p>
<p>This is not to say, of course, that ACEs cause people to be sexually violent, or that most people who experience ACEs become sex offenders. But it does suggest that ACEs might contribute to the conditions that encourage coercive sexual behavior.  Levenson, Willis, and Prescott point out, “an adverse family environment is a fertile breeding ground for sexual offending. Abuse, neglect, and family dysfunction often lead to mistrust, hostility, and insecure attachment, which then contribute to social rejection, loneliness, negative peer associations, and delinquent behavior.”</p>
<p>So what does this mean for sexual violence prevention professionals? How can we think about addressing the broader social contexts that lead to sexual violence? And how might incorporating responses to these traumatic experiences in sex offender treatment programs help prevent recidivism and additional sexual violence?</p>
<p><strong>Full Citation:</strong> Levenson, J., Willis, G., &amp; Prescott, D. Adverse childhood experiences in the lives of male sex offenders: Implications for trauma-informed care. <em>Sex Abuse. </em>Epublished: May 28, 2014.</p>
<p><strong>Link:</strong> <a href="http://sax.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/05/27/1079063214535819" target="_blank" class="broken_link">http://sax.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/05/27/1079063214535819</a></p>
<p><strong>Full Abstract:</strong> &#8220;This study explored the prevalence of childhood trauma in a sample of male sexual offenders (<em>N</em> = 679) using the Adverse Childhood Experience (ACE) scale. Compared with males in the general population, sex offenders had more than 3 times the odds of child sexual abuse (CSA), nearly twice the odds of physical abuse, 13 times the odds of verbal abuse, and more than 4 times the odds of emotional neglect and coming from a broken home. Less than 16% endorsed zero ACEs and nearly half endorsed four or more. Multiple maltreatments often co-occurred with other types of household dysfunction, suggesting that many sex offenders were raised within a disordered social environment. Higher ACE scores were associated with higher risk scores. By enhancing our understanding of the frequency and correlates of early adverse experiences, we can better devise trauma-informed interventions that respond to the clinical needs of sex offender clients.&#8221;</p>
]]></content:encoded>
