		<item>
		<title>The research says&#8230;</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/05/the-research-says/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/05/the-research-says/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Leona Smith Di Faustino]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 13 May 2014 21:25:28 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence based practice]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence based prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Leona Smith di Faustino]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18748</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[I believe that one can rarely go about their work today in our field without hearing the phrases, &#8220;The research&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/stack-of-papers.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft wp-image-18752 size-thumbnail" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/stack-of-papers-150x150.jpg?x47887" alt="stack of papers" width="150" height="150" /></a>I believe that one can rarely go about their work today in our field without hearing the phrases, &#8220;The research says&#8230;&#8221; or &#8220;What we know&#8230;&#8221;. I&#8217;ve found this experience of research being used as a tool to explain an absolute to be very fascinating. Let me be clear, I am the first person to find value in researching a process, concept, ideal, or mechanism for change. I am not in favor of being back in a place where, &#8220;This is how it&#8217;s been done&#8221;, was used to promote programs that are ineffective for communities. What I&#8217;ve become weary of is research becoming sacrosanct and critical analysis of the process one uses to gather data and the subsequent body of research created not getting questioned. This is of course in part influenced by the fact that as a Social Worker I&#8217;ve worked in communities research has not touched. Instead, models of practice have been imposed upon them that are not reflective of their unique socioeconomic and cultural experiences.</p>
<p>What I&#8217;d like to hear is a combination of what research may imply and what information still needs to be gathered. I&#8217;d love to have a conversation in which someone points out not only the data that has been gathered but what still needs to found out. I&#8217;d dance a little jig of happiness if the next time an evidence based curriculum was referred to, folks would acknowledge &#8220;perfect&#8221; models can&#8217;t always be applied to society&#8217;s not so &#8220;perfect&#8221; people. I don&#8217;t want to dismantle the whole system, I&#8217;d just like to make sure what the research is saying has meaning for everyone.</p>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/05/the-research-says/feed/</wfw:commentRss>
			<slash:comments>0</slash:comments>
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">18748</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>Sexual violence as normalized phenomena: A new study</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/04/sexual-violence-as-normalized-phenomena-a-new-study/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2014/04/sexual-violence-as-normalized-phenomena-a-new-study/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 21 Apr 2014 17:39:29 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[normalized sexual violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[norms]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18695</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A new study confirms what we have known for a long time: sexual violence is a normalized phenomenon, especially among&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.33.30-AM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-18696" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.33.30-AM.png?x47887" alt="Gender &amp; Society cover" width="129" height="197" /></a><a href="http://gas.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/02/28/0891243214526468.full?keytype=ref&amp;siteid=spgas&amp;ijkey=1zjS.dsfVDs32" target="_blank" class="broken_link">A new study</a> confirms what we have known for a long time: sexual violence is a normalized phenomenon, especially among young women.  In short, the study shows that young women make sense of the pervasive violence in their lives by framing it as normal, as no big deal.</p>
<blockquote><p>Young women overwhelmingly depicted boys and men as <em>natural</em> sexual aggressors, pointing to one of the main tenets of compulsory heterosexuality.</p></blockquote>
<p>The article discusses the findings through the lens of compulsory heterosexuality and heteronormativity – “traditional gender arrangements, beliefs, and behavior [that] reinforce women’s sexual subordination to men.”  Importantly, it reviews the discourse on children, sexuality, and sexual abuse since the 1970s, demonstrating how such normalization of violence came to be.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.44.51-AM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignright size-medium wp-image-18700" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.44.51-AM-300x45.png?x47887" alt="quote" width="300" height="45" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.44.51-AM-300x45.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Screen-Shot-2014-04-21-at-10.44.51-AM.png 551w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a>Study methodology includes audio-videotaped forensic interviews with 23 girls seen for reported cases of child sexual abuse, an essential point about the research.  This provides implications for sexual violence prevention, especially as it relates to the prevention of child sexual abuse.  Indeed, to create communities free of sexual violence, child sexual abuse must be addressed, and vice versa.</p>
<p>____________________________________________________________________________</p>
<p><strong>Article Link: </strong><a href="http://gas.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/02/28/0891243214526468.full?keytype=ref&amp;siteid=spgas&amp;ijkey=1zjS.dsfVDs32" target="_blank" class="broken_link">http://gas.sagepub.com/content/early/2014/02/28/0891243214526468.full?keytype=ref&amp;siteid=spgas&amp;ijkey=1zjS.dsfVDs32</a></p>
<p><strong>Abstract: </strong></p>
<p>Despite high rates of gendered violence among youth, very few young women report these incidents to authority figures. This study moves the discussion from the question of why young women do not report them toward how violence is produced, maintained, and normalized among youth. The girls in this study often did not name what law, researchers, and educators commonly identify as sexual harassment and abuse. How then, do girls name and make sense of victimization? Exploring violence via the lens of compulsory heterosexuality highlights the relational dynamics at play in this naming process. Forensic interviews with youth revealed patterns of heteronormative scripts appropriated to make sense of everyday harassment, violence, coercion, and consent. Findings inform discussions about the links between dominant discourses and sexual subjectivities as we try to better understand why many regard violence a normal part of life.</p>
<p><strong>APA Citation:</strong></p>
<p>Hlavka, H.R. (2014). Normalizing sexual violence: Young women account for harassment and abuse. <em>Gender &amp; Society</em>, <em>28(2)</em>, 1-22. DOI: 10.1177/0891243214526468</p>
]]></content:encoded>

					<description><![CDATA[Wednesday, November 13, 2013 11 AM to 12:30 PM Pacific Time (2 PM to 3:30 PM Eastern) As we seek&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<h2 style="text-align: center;"><span style="color: #ff0000;"><a href="http:/ilinc.preventconnect.org/2013/11-13_Prevalence_of_adolescent_sexual_violence_perpetration" target="_blank"><img loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-18307 aligncenter" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/08/recordingbutton.jpg?x47887" alt="Click here to access recordinf" width="194" height="125"/></a></span></h2>
<p style="text-align: center;"><strong>Wednesday, November 13, 2013</strong><br />
11 AM to 12:30 PM Pacific Time<br />
(2 PM to 3:30 PM Eastern)</p>
<div>
<p>As we seek strategies to prevent rape, we need to examine not only how to prevent sexual violence victimization, but we need strategies to prevent sexual violence perpetration. And to develop strategies to prevent sexual violence perpetration, we need to know about the perpetrators themselves. Yet, little information is known about youth perpetrators. A <a href="http://archpedi.jamanetwork.com/article.aspx?articleid=1748355" target="_blank">recent study in JAMA Pediatrics</a> provides new insight. The study found that nearly 1 in 10 youths reported some type of sexual violence perpetration in their lifetimes. Join one of the study&#8217;s authors to discuss the study and to explore implications for prevention.</p>
</div>
<p><span id="more-18336"></span></p>
<hr />
<figure id="attachment_18337" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-18337" style="width: 180px" class="wp-caption alignright"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/michele-ybarra.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class=" wp-image-18337 " src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/michele-ybarra-300x200.jpg?x47887" alt="Michele Ybarra" width="180" height="120" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/michele-ybarra-300x200.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/michele-ybarra-1024x685.jpg 1024w" sizes="(max-width: 180px) 100vw, 180px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-18337" class="wp-caption-text">Michele Ybarra</figcaption></figure>
<p><strong>Host:</strong> David Lee and Ashley Maier, <a href="http://calcasa.org" target="_blank">CALCASA</a>, <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org" target="_blank">PreventConnect</a></p>
<p><strong>Presenters:&nbsp;</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Michele Ybarra, <a href="http://innovativepublichealth.org/" target="_blank">Center for&nbsp;<em>Innovative</em>&nbsp;Public Health Research</a></li>

		<item>
		<title>Reducing resistance to social change: The ironic impact of activists</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/10/reducing-resistance-to-social-change-the-ironic-impact-of-activists/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/10/reducing-resistance-to-social-change-the-ironic-impact-of-activists/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 18 Oct 2013 23:11:36 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[activists]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[feminist]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[feminists]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[impact]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[ironic impact]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[resistance]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social change]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[stereotypes]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18322</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Sexual and domestic violence prevention work is social change work.  To successfully create social change we must also overcome resistance&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Screen-Shot-2013-10-09-at-4.42.50-PM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-18323" style="margin: 5px;" alt="Cover of European Journal of Social Psychology" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Screen-Shot-2013-10-09-at-4.42.50-PM.png?x47887" width="95" height="137" /></a>Sexual and domestic violence prevention work is social change work.  To successfully create social change we must also overcome resistance to it.  Attempting to understand the resistance, studies typically look at individual perceptions of the issues addressed, attitudes towards social change, and personality traits, but <a href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ejsp.1983/abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">a new study</a> in the European Journal of Social Psychology takes a different approach, turning the lens to activists:</p>
<blockquote><p>Because activists, by definition, strive to effect change by publicly criticizing mainstream practices, they may be seen as hostile, unconventional and unpleasant. This tendency to associate activists with negative stereotypes may ultimately reduce individuals’  willingness to affiliate with activists and adopt the pro-change behaviours that activists espouse.</p></blockquote>
<p>This five-part study found that participants who held negative perceptions of activists were less willing to affiliate with them and to adopt the behaviors they promoted.  For example, one portion of the study that focused specifically on feminist activists found:</p>
<blockquote><p>&#8230;participants were less motivated to adopt pro-equality behaviours when these behaviours were ostensibly advocated by the <b>‘</b> typical<b>’ </b> feminist rather than when these same behaviours were advocated by the <b>‘</b> atypical<b>’ </b> feminist or the undefined target, because participants were more likely to associate the <b>‘</b> typical<b>’ </b> feminist with negative stereotypical traits. Thus, despite aggressively promoting social change, <b>‘</b> typical<b>’ </b>activists may, ironically, undermine individuals<b>’ </b> motivation to engage in pro-change behaviours.</p></blockquote>
<p>Stereotypes played a big role in this research; they fostered negative perceptions of activists.  Most often, activists who were identified as &#8220;typical&#8221; (and therefore negatively perceived) were  viewed as eccentric and militant or aggressive.</p>
<blockquote><p>For many activists, the willingness to take a radical stand without regard for mainstream sensibilities is a point of pride&#8230;Ironically and despite good intentions, therefore, the very individuals who are most actively engaged in promoting social change may inadvertently alienate members of the public and reduce pro-change motivation.</p></blockquote>
<p>It is indeed ironic that the our very passion as activists may diminish our effectiveness in creating change and engaging others.  As disappointing as this is, this research acknowledges the difficulties many of us have faced in making our messages engaging to our audiences, shifting the lens to provide additional insight into barriers we may encounter.</p>
<p id="citation">Article citation: Bashir, N. Y., Lockwood, P., Chasteen, A. L., Nadolny, D. and Noyes, I. (2013), The ironic impact of activists: Negative stereotypes reduce social change influence. <em>European Journal of Social </em><i>Psychology</i>. doi: 10.1002/ejsp.1983</p>
<p>Click <a href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ejsp.1983/abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">here</a> to access the abstract.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Understanding rape perpetration to prevent rape perpetration</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/10/understanding-rape-perpetration-to-prevent-rape-perpetration/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/10/understanding-rape-perpetration-to-prevent-rape-perpetration/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Lee]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 03 Oct 2013 14:24:57 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Lancet]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Rachel Jewkes]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[sexual violence perpetration]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[UN]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18313</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[As we seeking strategies to prevent rape, we need to examine not only how to prevent sexual violence victimization, but &#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Lancet-global-Health.gif?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-18314" alt="Lancet GLobal Health covet" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Lancet-global-Health.gif?x47887" width="112" height="150" /></a>As we seeking strategies to prevent rape, we need to examine not only how to prevent sexual violence victimization, but  we need strategies to prevent sexual violence perpetration. And to develop strategies to prevent sexual violence perpetration, we need to know about the perpetrators themselves.</p>
<p>In the <a href="http://www.thelancet.com/journals/langlo/article/PIIS2214-109X%2813%2970074-3/abstract">recently released study published in the journal Lancet Global Health</a>, the researchers examined prevalence and factors associated with perpetration using data from interviews with 10,178 men from six countries in Asian and the Pacific.</p>
<p>The findings are disheartening: 24% of men committed rape against a partner or non-partner. (There was great variance among the countries ranging 11.1-60.7%).  In this research we hear from people who perpetrate rape themselves. Men who acknowledged that they had committed rape gave reasons for their actions:</p>
<blockquote><p>Of those who had raped a nonpartner woman (but not a man or a partner), the most common reason for the most recent rape expressed sexual entitlement (statements endorsed by 73% of men across the region; table 2), followed by entertainment seeking (59%), anger or punishment (38%), and alcohol or substance use (27%; table 2).</p></blockquote>
<p>The researchers also asked about the consequences of their action:</p>
<blockquote><p>…only 55% of men had felt guilty, and 23% had been sent to prison for rape of a partner or non-partner woman, or man (table 2), but this proportion varied from 2% (Sri Lanka) to 52% (Papua New Guinea), where traditional rape punishments are used, with short-term detention by the police in prison until traditional preparations have been made.</p></blockquote>
<p>While these samples are not nationally representative of those countries, nor able to be applied to the population, this provides  a stark picture of men who rape. Research exploring perpetration is necessary to understand how we can create change.</p>
<p>The researchers described the study as about “family and health” and. Instead of asking questions like “Have you ever raped someone? That asked behaviorally specific questions:</p>
<blockquote><p>The word rape was not used in the questionnaire; instead, it was operationalised through responses to questions about specific acts. Two questions about nonpartner rape asked about having “forced a woman who was not your wife or girlfriend at the time to have sex” or having “had sex with a woman who was too drunk or drugged to indicate whether she wanted it”, and how often the male interviewees had committed such acts. Two additional questions asked about having done these acts with other men (multiple perpetrator rape).</p></blockquote>
<p>For prevention practitioners, this type of research helps us understand perpetration in order to create prevention efforts that can make the necessary changes in communities that will contribute to preventing rape.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.partners4prevention.org" class="broken_link">Partners for Prevention</a> has posted data visualization graphics about this study <a href="http://www.partners4prevention.org/about-prevention/research/men-and-violence-study" class="broken_link">here</a>. Here is one of them:</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/gender-inequality.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="aligncenter  wp-image-18316" alt="Violence against women is fundamentally about gender inequality. Men who frequently quarreled and were controlling over their partner, paid for sex and had gender-inequitable attitudes were more likely to use violence against women." src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/gender-inequality.jpg?x47887" width="416" height="239" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/gender-inequality.jpg 594w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/gender-inequality-300x172.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 416px) 100vw, 416px" /></a></p>
<p>Here is the full citation of the study:</p>
<blockquote><p>Rachel Jewkes, Emma Fulu, Tim Roselli, Claudia Garcia-Moreno, on behalf of the UN Multi-country Cross-sectional Study on Men and Violence research team (2013) Prevalence of and factors associated with male perpetration of intimate partner violence: findings from the UN Multi-country Cross-sectional Study on Men and Violence in Asia and the Pacific, <strong>The Lancet Global Health, </strong>Volume 1, Issue 4, Pages e187 &#8211; e207, October 2013,  Click <a href="http://www.thelancet.com/journals/langlo/article/PIIS2214-109X%2813%2970074-3/abstract">here</a> for the abstract.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Dating violence among Latino adolescents: Implications for prevention</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/07/dating-violence-among-latino-adolescents-implications-for-prevention/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/07/dating-violence-among-latino-adolescents-implications-for-prevention/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Lee]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 29 Jul 2013 16:40:50 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Latino]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[NIJ]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[teen dating violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Teen Dating Violence Prevention]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18219</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[One out of five Latino adolescents experienced teen dating violence according to the the newly released Dating Violence Among Latino&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft  wp-image-18221" alt="Overall, findings from DAVILA show that 19.5% of Latino teens experienced dating violence in the past year- equivalent to 1 in 5 Latino adolescents." src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA-1024x530.png?x47887" width="344" height="178" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA-1024x530.png 1024w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA-300x155.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA.png 1303w" sizes="(max-width: 344px) 100vw, 344px" /></a>One out of five Latino adolescents experienced teen dating violence according to the the newly released <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA-General-Population-Report.pdf?x47887">Dating Violence Among Latino Adolescence Study (DAVILA)</a>. This National Institute of Justice funded study conducted interviews with a national sample of 1525 Latino adolescents. 6.6% had experienced physical dating violence, 5.6% had experienced sexual dating violence, 1.0% had experienced stalking by a dating partner, and 14.8% had experienced psychological dating violence.</p>
<p>This study found that males reported higher rates of victimization than girls reported.  The researchers used questions from the Conflict Tactics Scale 2 which has been criticized. (see the <a href="http://new.vawnet.org/print-document.php?doc_id=388&amp;find_type=web_desc_AR" class="broken_link">VAWnet paper Measuring the Extent of Woman Abuse in Intimate Heterosexual Relationships: A Critique of the Conflict Tactics Scales</a> for details.)</p>
<p>In their findings, authors Chiara Sabina and Carlos Cuevas made recommendations for prevention among Latino adolescents:</p>
<blockquote><p>&#8230;prevention efforts should focus on this form of dating violence and equip youth to identify and confront controlling actions, put-downs and jealously, for example. <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1359178912000237">Shorey (2012)</a> recommends a psychoeducational component focused on psychological aggression and communication skills be included in prevention efforts. These programs should be adapted to the Latino population including language, idioms, how discrimination may play a role in dating violence, and the importance of family.</p></blockquote>
<p>The full report can be found <a href="https://ncjrs.gov/pdffiles1/nij/grants/242775.pdf">here</a> and a summary of the report is <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/07/DAVILA-General-Population-Report.pdf?x47887">here</a>.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>New report on teens and social media</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-report-on-teens-and-social-media/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-report-on-teens-and-social-media/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 22 May 2013 17:52:07 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Facebook]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[privacy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social media]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[teens]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[youth]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18059</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[The Pew Research Center has just released a new report entitled Teens, Social Media, and Privacy.  While focused on privacy,&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/Screen-Shot-2013-05-22-at-10.50.32-AM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft  wp-image-18060" style="margin: 5px;" alt="infographic of facebook connections" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/Screen-Shot-2013-05-22-at-10.50.32-AM.png?x47887" width="322" height="285" /></a>The Pew Research Center has just released a new report entitled <i><a href="http://www.pewinternet.org/Reports/2013/Teens-Social-Media-And-Privacy.aspx" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Teens, Social Media, and Privacy</a>.</i>  While focused on privacy, it provides a range of useful information, such as the fact that Facebook was the most popular social medial tool among teens in the study.</p>
<p>Regarding privacy, the report shows that teens are sharing more information than ever online, but they are also aware of and skilled in using privacy settings on social media sites.  For example, 56% of teens reported that it was “not difficult at all” to manage the privacy settings on their Facebook pages.  Another 33% said doing so was “not too difficult.”</p>
<p>The report provides a number of interesting adjunctive materials, including two interactive features and four infographics.  And while it both confirms our movement’s concerns about the dangers of social media, it also demonstrates that teens are a lot more social media and tech saavy than we tend to give them credit for.  They know privacy is an issue and choose various ways to deal with it.</p>
<blockquote><p>Facebook is a major center of teenage social interactions, both with the positives of friendship and social support and the negatives of drama and social expectations.</p></blockquote>
<p>It’s clear from the report that teens both like and hate social media sites, especially Facebook. It defines their social lives and, in some cases, feels like an obligation.  So as we consider how to <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/10/prevention-how-state-local-health-departments-can-capitalize-on-social-media/" target="_blank">use social media</a> to <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/08/bystander-intervention-using-social-media-to-build-bystander-action-to-prevent-violence/" target="_blank">reach teens</a>, and as the trends change daily, reports like this become evermore important.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.pewinternet.org/Reports/2013/Teens-Social-Media-And-Privacy.aspx" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Click here</a> to access the full report and related materials. For tips on how to use media and technology in sexual and domestic violence prevention, explore <a href="http://learn.preventconnect.org/course/view.php?id=24" target="_blank" class="broken_link">this online course</a>.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>New Book: Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-book-strengthening-systems-to-prevent-intimate-partner-and-sexual-violence/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-book-strengthening-systems-to-prevent-intimate-partner-and-sexual-violence/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 16 May 2013 23:15:12 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[CDC]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[DELTA]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[EMPOWER]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18054</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence is a collection of nine articles summarizing the experience of the&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><i><a href="http://www.routledge.com/books/details/9780415522885/" target="_blank"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft  wp-image-18055" alt="book cover" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/strengtheningsystemscdcbook.jpg?x47887" width="180" height="180" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/strengtheningsystemscdcbook.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/strengtheningsystemscdcbook-150x150.jpg 150w" sizes="(max-width: 180px) 100vw, 180px" /></a><a href="http://www.routledge.com/books/details/9780415522885/" target="_blank">Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence</a></i> is a collection of nine articles summarizing the experience of the DELTA and EMPOWER programs.  Both <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/delta/" target="_blank">DELTA</a> (Domestic Violence Prevention Enhancements and Leadership Through Alliances) and <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/sexualviolence/empower/index.html" target="_blank" class="broken_link">EMPOWER</a> (Enhancing and Making Programs Work to End Rape) began in the 2000s with the purpose of enhancing the primary prevention of intimate partner violence and sexual violence, using a public health approach.  The articles discuss a range of topics related to the programs, including evaluation, capacity building and theory.  Several case studies demonstrate the lessons learned from the project, which include five components of a new approach to prevention planning:</p>
<ul>
<li>The use of empowerment evaluation as a set of guiding principles</li>
<li>Operating from an intentional and rich theory base</li>
<li>Experimenting with strategies to promote inclusion</li>
<li>Application of a data driven planning process</li>
<li>The importance of building capacity</li>
</ul>
<p>While specific to two CDC grant programs, <i>Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence</i>’s findings, examples, and insights can inform anyone doing sexual and domestic violence prevention work, at state and local levels.  For example, <i>Youth Action Strategies in the Primary Prevention of Teen Dating Violence</i> offers a comprehensive look at a local project to prevent teen dating violence in Wisconsin.</p>
<p>Click <a href="http://www.routledge.com/books/details/9780415522885/" target="_blank">here</a> to access the book.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;">Citation</span></p>
<p>Conway, P., Cox, P.J., Cook-Craig, P.G., Ortega, S., &amp; Armstead, T.L. (Eds.). (2013). <i>Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner Violence and Sexual Violence. </i>New York, NY. Routledge.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;">Description of the book (from Routledge)</span></p>
<blockquote><p>Over the past 25 years, developing coordinated responses to intimate partner violence and sexual violence has improved both perpetrator accountability, and victim safety and self-determination. However, preventing intimate partner violence and sexual violence from occurring is beyond the ability of any one type of organization. Preventing this violence requires a network of individuals, groups and organizations who coordinate and assess their efforts on an ongoing basis.</p>
<p>This volume provides theoretical and practical guidance for the development of state and local prevention systems that hold the potential to eliminate persistent social problems. The development of prevention systems was informed by the data-driven public health model, systems theory and the ecological systems perspective. <i>Strengthening Systems to Prevent Intimate Partner Violence and Sexual Violence</i> offers guidance on how to gain participation of the right partners in developing a prevention system, and how to focus the work of that system on the critical areas of planning, implementation and capacity building. The guidance, resources and experience shared in this important collection will be invaluable to all those working towards the prevention of intimate partner violence and sexual violence.</p>
<p>This book is based on a special issue of the <i>Journal of Family Social Work</i>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>New research and a rapey news cycle: Undoing toxic masculinity</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-research-and-a-rapey-news-cycle-undoing-toxic-masculinity/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/05/new-research-and-a-rapey-news-cycle-undoing-toxic-masculinity/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 09 May 2013 19:25:54 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Cleveland]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Don't Be That Guy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Huffington Post]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Jaclyn Friedman]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[kidnappings]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[military]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=18033</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Today on her Facebook page, Jaclyn Friedman referred to the particularly “rapey news cycle” of late.  And that it is. &#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.democracynow.org/2013/5/9/behind_the_cleveland_kidnappings_a_culture" target="_blank"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-medium wp-image-18034" style="margin: 5px;" alt="Jaclyn Friedman on TV" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/Screen-Shot-2013-05-09-at-12.19.34-PM-300x167.png?x47887" width="300" height="167" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/Screen-Shot-2013-05-09-at-12.19.34-PM-300x167.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/Screen-Shot-2013-05-09-at-12.19.34-PM.png 634w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a>Today on her <a href="https://www.facebook.com/jaclynf?fref=ts">Facebook page</a>, Jaclyn Friedman referred to the particularly “rapey news cycle” of late.  And that it is.  From <a href="http://www.cbc.ca/thecurrent/episode/2013/05/09/sexual-assault-and-the-us-military/">sexual assault in the military</a>, to the <a href="http://www.democracynow.org/2013/5/9/behind_the_cleveland_kidnappings_a_culture">Cleveland kidnappings</a>, these stories have generated a renewed look at norms around masculinity and how they contribute to rape culture, a culture that ultimately produces the events that lead to such “rapey news cycles.”</p>
<p>In <a href="http://www.democracynow.org/2013/5/9/behind_the_cleveland_kidnappings_a_culture">an interview</a> about the Cleveland kidnappings and rapes, Friedman stated that creating a world free of sexual violence, “requires cultural change and undoing toxic masculinity.”  Then, in <a href="http://www.cbc.ca/thecurrent/episode/2013/05/09/sexual-assault-and-the-us-military/">an interview</a> about the high rates of sexual assault in the military, she went on to say:</p>
<blockquote><p>The dominant paradigm of masculinity is that we’re living in and the culture is defined as masculinity is derived from power over women and it makes people very uncomfortable to suggest we have to do the work of creating new ways to create masculinity that’s power <i>with</i> women…</p></blockquote>
<p>Indeed, in the same interview, guests Angela Marie McDonald, discussing Vancouver’s <a href="http://www.theviolencestopshere.ca/dbtg.php" class="broken_link">Don’t Be That Guy</a> campaign, and <a href="http://www.politico.com/">Politico</a>’s Juana Summers call for culture change when asked just how one can prevent sexual violence in institutions such as the military.  “It’s very hard to culturally change an institution.  And that part can’t come from lawmakers,” Summers says about the military.</p>
<p>And <a href="http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11199-013-0268-1">new research</a> confirms the need for culture change, particularly around notions about masculinity.  Psychologists from the University of Manitoba studied advertisements in men’s magazines and found that they promoted the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Danger is exciting</li>
<li>Toughness is a form of emotional self-control</li>
<li>Violence is manly</li>
<li>It’s fine to be callous about women and sex</li>
</ul>
<p>These are hypermasculine beliefs, and as <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2013/05/07/be-a-man-macho-hypermasculine-advertising_n_3230402.html">Huffington Post points out</a>, “previous research has linked these beliefs to social and medical problems in men,” including violence against women.</p>
<p>So what can be done?  The news media has picked up on a key component of prevention violence against women – examining and changing harmful notions about what it means to be a man.  Read more about innovative initiatives to do just that on <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/?s=masculinity">our blog</a>, learn about these norms on <a href="http://learn.preventconnect.org/course/view.php?id=18" class="broken_link">our eLearning site</a>, watch one of many <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/11/promoting-healthy-masculinity/">web conferences</a> about healthy masculinity, and <i>continue the conversation</i>.</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>How effective are campus-based gender violence prevention programs?</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2013/01/how-effective-are-campus-based-gender-violence-prevention-programs/</link>

		<pubDate>Wed, 30 Jan 2013 19:24:03 +0000</pubDate>

					<description><![CDATA[VAWnet, the National Online Resource Center on Violence Against Women  just released a publication entitled, The Evaluation of Campus-Based Gender&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/08/VAWnet.gif?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-17318" alt="VAWnet" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/08/VAWnet.gif?x47887" width="228" height="91" /></a><a href="http://www.vawnet.org">VAWnet, the National Online Resource Center on Violence Against Women</a>  just released a publication entitled, <a href="http://www.google.com/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=&amp;esrc=s&amp;source=web&amp;cd=1&amp;cad=rja&amp;ved=0CDIQFjAA&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.vawnet.org%2FAssoc_Files_VAWnet%2FAR_EvaluationCampusProgramming.pdf&amp;ei=oHMJUdG1EaiU2gXLsoHQAQ&amp;usg=AFQjCNEAE0_IaJf0JufeP7Zm8ck2P79niw&amp;bvm=bv.41642243,d.b2I" target="_blank">The Evaluation of Campus-Based Gender Violence Prevention Programming: What We Know about Program Effectiveness and Implication for Practitioners </a>by Roberta E. Gibbons. It covers the effectiveness of various programs for reducing sexual and domestic violence on campus, such as: risk reduction/self-defense programs, empathy building programs, rape awareness/attitude change programs and bystander programs. <span id="more-17814"></span></p>
<p>Findings include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Programs have been effective in increasing knowledge and decreasing rape supportive attitudes, especially in the short term.</li>
<li>Dosage matters. Longer and more frequent exposures to interventions result in greater outcomes.</li>
<li>Bystander programs have demonstrated link between change in attitudes an change in behavior.</li>
</ul>
<p>To read the full article <a href="http://www.google.com/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=&amp;esrc=s&amp;source=web&amp;cd=1&amp;cad=rja&amp;ved=0CDIQFjAA&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.vawnet.org%2FAssoc_Files_VAWnet%2FAR_EvaluationCampusProgramming.pdf&amp;ei=oHMJUdG1EaiU2gXLsoHQAQ&amp;usg=AFQjCNEAE0_IaJf0JufeP7Zm8ck2P79niw&amp;bvm=bv.41642243,d.b2I" target="_blank">click here. </a></p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>A 45 year follow-up study on child sexual abuse</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/12/a-45-year-follow-up-study-on-child-sexual-abuse/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/12/a-45-year-follow-up-study-on-child-sexual-abuse/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Lee]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 11 Dec 2012 16:39:51 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[child sexual abuse]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[James Ogloff]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Joan Tabachnick]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[NEARI]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[NEARI Press]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Trends and Issues in Crime and Criminal Justice]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17705</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[The December 2012 edition of NEARI News highlights a recent study, Child sexual abuse and subsequent offending and victimisation: A 45&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/12/trends1.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-medium wp-image-17707" title="trends" alt="Trends and Issues in Crime and Criminal Justice masthead" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/12/trends1-300x194.png?x47887" width="300" height="194" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/12/trends1-300x194.png 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/12/trends1.png 934w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a>The December 2012 edition of <a href="http://www.nearipress.org/newsletter">NEARI News</a> highlights a recent study, <a href="http://www.aic.gov.au/publications/current%20series/tandi/421-440/tandi440.html" class="broken_link">Child sexual abuse and subsequent offending and victimisation: A 45 year follow-up study</a>, published in the journal <a href="http://www.aic.gov.au/publications/current%20series/tandi.html" class="broken_link">Trends and Issues in Crime and Criminal Justice</a>. In this study researchers followed 2,759 child sexual abuse victims for a period ranging from 13 to 44 years giving us a valuable understanding of the long-term effects of child sexual abuse.</p>
<p>Joan Tabachnick, David Prescott and Steven Bengis of NEARI Press summarize the research:</p>
<blockquote><p>The research clearly states that most children who are victimized by sexual abuse do not go on to sexually abuse others. However, the research findings did suggest that sexual victimization in boys over age 12 may be an important risk factor for this population.</p></blockquote>
<p>For prevention,  the NEARI News documents recommends universal education, especially for boys over twelve should be considered as an important prevention strategy.</p>
<p>See the <a href="http://www.nearipress.org/images/PDF/nearinewsletterdecember2012.pdf">entire edition of the December 2012 NEARI News</a> for more detailed analysis of the study.  A full citation and abstract of the study are included below.</p>
<blockquote><p>James RP Ogloff, Margaret C Cutajar, Emily Mann and Paul Mullen, Child sexual abuse and subsequent offending and victimisation: A 45 year follow-up study, <em>Trends &amp; issues in crime and criminal justice</em>,  no.440, Canberra: Australian Institute of Criminology, June 2012. Full text available at no cost <a href="http://www.aic.gov.au/documents/4/3/F/%7b43F84BC9-770B-41AC-A2AA-8F4B5AFFC1EB%7dtandi440.pdf" class="broken_link">here</a>.</p>
<p><strong>Abstract</strong>: Up to 30 percent of children experience childhood sexual abuse (CSA) and whether this impacts re-victimisation or offending as an adult has been the subject of numerous studies.</p>
<p>This study investigates whether a disproportionate number of CSA victims subsequently perpetrate offences and experience future victimisation compared with people who have not been sexually abused. In a sample of 2,759 CSA victims who were abused between 1964 and 1995, it was found CSA victims were almost five times more likely than the general population to be charged with any offence than their non-abused counterparts, with strongest associations found for sexual and violent offences. CSA victims were also more likely to have been victims of crime, particularly crimes of a sexual or violent nature.</p>
<p>This research highlights the need for therapeutic interventions targeted at adolescent male CSA victims, particularly with regard to offender treatment programs, where many programs currently do not allow for exploration of offenders’ own sexual victimisation.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Top innovations in prevention</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/12/top-innovations-in-prevention/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/12/top-innovations-in-prevention/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Lee]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 04 Dec 2012 18:39:37 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[CDC]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[CDC Injury Center]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[engaging boys]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[engaging men]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Jourrnal of Safety Research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[NSVRC]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[VAWnet]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[VetoViolence]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17687</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[As the National Center for Injury Prevention and Control of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention celebrates its 20th &#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/03/CDC_20ann_btn2_120w.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-16879" title="CDC NCIPC 20 Year 120w" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/03/CDC_20ann_btn2_120w.png?x47887" alt="" width="120" height="96" /></a>As the <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/injury/index.html">National Center for Injury Prevention and Control</a> of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention celebrates its <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/injury/anniversary/index.html" class="broken_link">20th  anniversary</a>, a recent article in the <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/journal/00224375">Journal of Safety Research</a> lists the <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022437512000473">Top 20 violence and injury practice innovations since 1992</a>.  PreventConnect is pleased to be featured in this article.</p>
<p>Here are some of the top innovations related to sexual and domestic violence prevention:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Connecting and Training Practitioners Online</strong>: With advances in technology, more an more resources are available online to build the capacity of prevention practitioners. PreventConnect is recognized as one of the online resources that has built a community of prevention practitioners. Other highlights included CDC’s <a href="http://www.vetoviolence.org" class="broken_link">VetoViolence</a> eLearning site, <a href="http://www.vawnet.org">VAWnet</a> and <a href="http://www.nsvrc.org">National Sexual Violence Resource Center</a>.</li>
<li><strong>Moving Upstream in Sexual and Intimate Partner Violence Prevention – RPE and DELTA</strong>.  IN the last 10 years CDC’s leading primary prevention initiatives have demonstrated the value of advancing primary prevention efforts. The <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science?_ob=RedirectURL&amp;_method=externObjLink&amp;_locator=url&amp;_issn=00224375&amp;_origin=article&amp;_zone=art_page&amp;_plusSign=%2B&amp;_targetURL=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdc.gov%2Fviolenceprevention%2Frpe%2Findex.html" class="broken_link">Rape Prevention Education Program</a> (RPE) and <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science?_ob=RedirectURL&amp;_method=externObjLink&amp;_locator=url&amp;_issn=00224375&amp;_origin=article&amp;_zone=art_page&amp;_plusSign=%2B&amp;_targetURL=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdc.gov%2Fviolenceprevention%2Fdelta%2Findex.html" class="broken_link">The Domestic Violence Prevention Enhancement and Leadership Through Alliances Program</a> (DELTA) aim to increase primary prevention of intimate partner (IPV) and sexual violence (SV) through capacity-building in states and communities.<strong></strong></li>
<li><strong>Engaging Boys and Men to Prevent Violence Against Women. </strong>Many organizations have developed approaches that engage boys and men as part of the solution to violence against women, and prevention more broadly, Examples include <a href="http://www.futureswithoutviolence.org/content/features/detail/811/" class="broken_link">Coaching Boys to Men</a>, <a href="http://www.mencanstoprape.org/The-Men-of-Strength-Club/" class="broken_link">Men of Strength Clubs</a> and organizations such as <a href="http://www.menstoppingviolence.org">Men Stopping Violence</a> and <a href="http://www.mensworkinc.com">MensWork</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Below is the full citation of the article. <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022437512000473">Click here</a> to get the full text.</p>
<p>Howard C. Kress, Rita Noonan,  Kimberley Freire, Angela Marr, Acasia Olson, <strong>Top 20 violence and injury practice innovations since 1992</strong>, <a title="Go to Journal of Safety Research on SciVerse ScienceDirect" href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/journal/00224375">Journal of Safety Research</a>, Volume 43, Issue 4, September 2012, Pages 257–263</p>
<blockquote><p><strong>Abstract</strong>: This article presents what the authors consider to be among the top 20 practice innovations since the inception of the National Center for Injury Prevention and Control in 1992. The innovations embody various characteristics of successful public health programs and have contributed to declines in violence, motor vehicle, residential fire, and other injury rates over the past 20 years. Taken together, these innovations have reduced the burden of violence and injury and have influenced current practice and practitioners in the United States and worldwide.</p></blockquote>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>TV can increase positive bystander behavior</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/10/tv-can-increase-positive-bystander-behavior/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/10/tv-can-increase-positive-bystander-behavior/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Alexis Marbach]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 23 Oct 2012 20:00:56 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bystander intervention]]></category>

		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>

					<description><![CDATA[A study recently released in the Journal of Health Communication analyzed tv crime dramas as a potentially useful venue for&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/Screen-shot-2012-10-23-at-12.47.25-PM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-17551" title="Screen shot 2012-10-23 at 12.47.25 PM" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/Screen-shot-2012-10-23-at-12.47.25-PM-150x150.png?x47887" alt="Image of a Remote Control" width="150" height="150" /></a>A study recently released in the <a href="http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/10810730.2012.688241?journalCode=uhcm20">Journal of Health Communication</a> analyzed tv crime dramas as a potentially useful venue for prevention messages including increasing intentions to intervene in a sexual assault. <a href="http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/10810730.2012.688241?journalCode=uhcm20">&#8220;Health Promotion Messages in Entertainment Media: Crime Drama Viewership and Intentions to Intervene in a Sexual Assault Situation&#8221;</a> suggests that &#8220;exposure to crime dramas was positively associated with intentions to intervene&#8221;. This finding took the instrumentality, rape myth acceptance, perceptions of peer&#8217;s willingness to intervene, and perceived confidence in abilities to intervene into account. Instrumentality was operationalized as confidence, decisiveness, independence, and helping behaviors.<span id="more-17549"></span></p>
<p>To learn more, visit the <a href="http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/10810730.2012.688241?journalCode=uhcm20">Journal of Health Communication.</a></p>
<h3>Citation</h3>
<p><a title="Journal of health communication." href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23020668#">J Health Commun.</a> 2012 Sep 28. [Epub ahead of print]. Health Promotion Messages in Entertainment Media: Crime Drama Viewership and Intentions to Intervene in a Sexual Assault Situation. <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Hust%20SJ%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Hust SJ</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Marett%20EG%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Marett EG</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Lei%20M%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Lei M</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Chang%20H%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Chang H</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Ren%20C%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Ren C</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=McNab%20AL%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">McNab AL</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Adams%20PM%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=23020668">Adams PM</a>.</p>
<h3>Source</h3>
<p>a Edward R. Murrow College of Communication, Washington State University , Pullman , Washington , USA.</p>
<div>
<h3>Abstract</h3>
<p>Popular crime dramas have tackled sensitive issues such as sexual assault with increasing frequency over the past 20 years. These popular programs increasingly demonstrate the emotional and physical effect of sexual assault on its victims, and in some instances they depict individuals being rewarded for intervening to prevent or stop an assault in progress. It is possible that this content could affect attitudes related to sexual assault prevention. However, no previous research has examined this possibility. In the fall 2008 semester, 508 undergraduates at a large northwestern university completed a questionnaire about media use and bystander intervention in a sexual assault situation. Results from hierarchical regressions lend support for the integrative model of behavioral prediction in that instrumentality, rape myth acceptance, perceived social norms, perceived efficacy related to intervening, and exposure to primetime crime dramas were associated with participants&#8217; intentions to intervene in a sexual assault. The results suggest that crime dramas may be a useful venue for prevention messages as exposure to crime dramas uniquely contributed to intentions to intervene in a sexual assault.</p>
<p><em>Photo courtesy of: http://www.flickr.com/photos/28478778@N05/5728485497/</em></p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Research on gender differences in prosocial behaviors</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/10/research-on-gender-differences-in-prosocial-behaviors/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/10/research-on-gender-differences-in-prosocial-behaviors/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 10 Oct 2012 21:33:03 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[engaging men]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[gender differences]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prosocial behavior]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[volunteering]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17511</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[A preventionist might be quick to pass off Gender Differences in the Correlates of Volunteering and Charitable Giving to a&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/photo.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft  wp-image-17512" title="gender symbols" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/photo.jpg?x47887" alt="" width="115" height="115" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/photo.jpg 320w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/photo-150x150.jpg 150w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/10/photo-300x300.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 115px) 100vw, 115px" /></a>A preventionist might be quick to pass off <em><a href="http://nvs.sagepub.com/content/early/2010/09/28/0899764010385949.abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Gender Differences in the Correlates of Volunteering and Charitable Giving</a></em> to a development director or volunteer coordinator, but that would be a mistake.  The article explores differences in prosocial behavior based on gender identity.  And what are we as preventionists working towards if not the promotion of prosocial behavior?</p>
<p>In the article, author Christopher Einolf theorizes that while women are more motivated to help than men, men’s greater social and human capital cancels this out, leading to little difference in men and women’s prosocial behaviors.  After examining the data, however, he concludes that overall, women are more prosocially motivated and men have only a small advantage in resources and social capital when it comes to volunteering and charitable giving.  Because men are not as proscially motivated as women, therefore, they may need more “hooks” to drawn them in for volunteering and charitable giving.</p>
<p>In reading this article, I thought of efforts to engage men in the prevention of sexual and domestic violence.  When local sexual and domestic violence programs, or other community-based organizations, work to engage men in their communities in the prevention of sexual and domestic violence, they are essentially asking for men’s volunteer time.  If they approach recruitment as they have for the countless women who have volunteered for the movement over the decades, they may be unsuccessful.  If, as this article posits, men are not as intrinsically motivated to volunteer for the cause as women are, they will have to do some very strategic thinking and planning to approach and engage men differently.</p>
<p>Here is the full citation and abstract:</p>
<blockquote><p>Einolf, C.J. (2001). Gender Differences in the Correlates of Volunteering and Charitable Giving. <em>Nonprofit and Volunteering Quarterly</em><em>, 40, </em>1092-1114.</p>
<p><a href="http://nvs.sagepub.com/content/early/2010/09/28/0899764010385949.abstract" target="_blank" class="broken_link">Click here</a> to see the article on the journal’s website.</p>
<p><strong>Abstract</strong></p>
<p>Psychological research has found that women score higher on most measures of the traits, motivations, and values that predict helping others, and women are more likely to help family and friends. However, sex differences in the institutional helping behaviors of volunteering and charitable giving are small. This article seeks to explain this apparent contradiction with the hypotheses that men have more resources and more social capital than women, which compensates for their lower level of motivation. The article tests these hypotheses using data from the 1995 Midlife in the United States (MIDUS) survey. The data show partial support for these hypotheses, as men score higher on measures of income, education, trust, and secular social networks. However, women have broader social networks through religious participation.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>New research: Assessing the Effects of Families for Safe Dates</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-assessing-the-effects-of-families-for-safe-dates/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-assessing-the-effects-of-families-for-safe-dates/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Lee]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 21 Sep 2012 20:29:38 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[evidence based prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[family]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Journal of Adolescent Health]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[teen dating violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Vangie Foshee]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17438</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[To prevent teen dating violence, we need to think about prevention efforts that go beyond classroom based strategies. It can&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<figure id="attachment_17440" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-17440" style="width: 300px" class="wp-caption alignleft"><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/FSD.jpg?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-17440" title="FSD" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/FSD-300x137.jpg?x47887" alt="Booklets from Families for Safe Dates" width="300" height="137" srcset="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/FSD-300x137.jpg 300w, http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/FSD.jpg 604w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></a><figcaption id="caption-attachment-17440" class="wp-caption-text">Booklets from Families for Safe Dates</figcaption></figure>
<p>To prevent teen dating violence, we need to think about prevention efforts that go beyond classroom based strategies. It can be hard to get into classrooms (especially to provide multiple sessions that we know are more likely to prevent violence than single sessions.)</p>
<p>How can we work with parents and families to prevent teen dating violence? In my experience as a prevention practitioner and as a parent, I know parents are less involved than they were when their children were younger. Just if we could find a way to help parents and families to make a difference&#8230;</p>
<p>In the October 2012 issue of the <em>Journal of Adolescent Health, </em>Vangie Foshee and her colleagues have an article <a href="http://www.jahonline.org/article/S1054-139X(11)00718-X/abstract">Assessing the Effects of Families for Safe Dates, a Family-Based Teen Dating Abuse Prevention Program</a> that evaluates a family based prevention effort.</p>
<p>You can learn more about this study when she joins the PreventConnect web conference <a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/involving-families-in-teen-dating-abuse-prevention/">Involving families in teen dating abuse prevention</a> on September 28, 2012.</p>
<p>Here is the full citation and abstract:</p>
<blockquote><p>Vangie A. Foshee, Heath Luz McNaughton Reyes, Susan T. Ennett, Jessica D. Cance, Karl E. Bauman, J. Michael Bowling (2012) Assessing the Effects of Families for Safe Dates, a Family-Based Teen Dating Abuse Prevention Program. <strong>Journal of Adolescent Health</strong>, Volume 51, Issue 4 , Pages 349-356, October 2012</p>
<p>Click <a href="http://www.jahonline.org/article/S1054-139X(11)00718-X/abstract">here to see the article on the journal&#8217;s web site</a>.</p>
<p><strong>Abstract</strong></p>
<p><strong>Purpose</strong>: To examine the effects of a family-based teen dating abuse prevention program, Families for Safe Dates, primarily on outcomes related to testing the conceptual underpinnings of the program including (1) factors motivating and facilitating caregiver engagement in teen dating abuse prevention activities, and 2) risk factors for teen dating abuse, and secondarily on dating abuse behaviors.</p>
<p><strong>Methods</strong>: Families were recruited nationwide using listed telephone numbers. Caregivers and teens completed baseline and 3-month follow-up telephone interviews (n = 324). Families randomly allocated to treatment condition received the Families for Safe Dates program including six mailed activity booklets followed-up by health educator telephone calls.</p>
<p><strong>Results</strong>: There were significant (&lt;.05) treatment effects in hypothesized directions on most of the factors motivating and facilitating caregiver engagement in teen dating abuse prevention activities including caregiver perceived severity of dating abuse, response efficacy for preventing dating abuse, self-efficacy for talking about dating abuse, knowledge of dating abuse, acceptance of dating abuse, communication skills with the teen, and belief in the importance of involvement in their male (but not female) teen&#8217;s dating. The latter effect was the only one moderated by sex of the teen. The targeted risk factor affected by the program was teen acceptance of dating abuse. Treatment was also significantly associated with less physical dating abuse victimization.</p>
<p><strong>Conclusions</strong>: Modifications to the program are warranted, but overall, the findings are very favorable for the first family-based teen dating abuse prevention program to be evaluated.</p></blockquote>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>New research on the health impact of being a bystander</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-on-the-health-impact-of-being-a-bystander/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-on-the-health-impact-of-being-a-bystander/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Alexis Marbach]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 13 Sep 2012 15:01:59 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Resources]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bystander]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[resources]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[resrouces]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17366</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[When I think for the benefits of bystander intervention, my thoughts initially turn to the positive impact that a bystander&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>When I think for the benefits of bystander intervention, my thoughts initially turn to the positive impact that a bystander can have for a victim (or potential victim). Recent research suggests that bystanders may experience separate and distinct health impacts that deserve attention and focus from health care professionals, parents, teachers, and support networks. In <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909907">&#8220;Morbidity among bystanders of bullying behavior at school: concepts, concerns, and clinical/research issues&#8221;, Ian Rivers</a> discusses the complexities of bystander behavior and how the social dynamics of bystander intervention can determine the long term sequele of an individual&#8217;s mental health. Rivers determined that &#8220;the mental health implications &#8230; vary according to the category or type of bystander a student becomes&#8221;, types ranging from those who support the bully or perpetrator to ones that actively fight for the rights of the victim. River&#8217;s conclusion:</p>
<blockquote><p>It is incumbent upon schools and those who administer schools to find ways of supporting the whole educational community rather than focusing their attention upon victims and perpetrators. Post-traumatic stress, internalized hostility, substance use, and suicide ideation are issues as commonly found among witnesses as they are among victims of assault, abuse, natural disasters, or man-made events<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909907"> (Rivers, 2012)</a></p></blockquote>
<p>What are ways in which you support a bystander not only as the intervene but after the incident?</p>
<p>To learn more:</p>
<address><img title="More..." src="http://calcasa.org/wp-includes/js/tinymce/plugins/wordpress/img/trans.gif" alt="" /></address>
<p><a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909907"><strong>Morbidity among bystanders of bullying behavior at school: concepts, concerns, and clinical/research issues.</strong></a></p>
<p><a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed?term=Rivers%20I%5BAuthor%5D&amp;cauthor=true&amp;cauthor_uid=22909907">Rivers I</a>.</p>
<p><a title="International journal of adolescent medicine and health." href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909907#">Int J Adolesc Med Health.</a> 2011 Nov 4;24(1):11-6. doi: 10.1515/ijamh.2012.003.</p>
<p>Source</p>
<p>School of Sport and Education, Brunel University, Uxbridge, UK. ian.rivers@brunel.ac.uk</p>
<div>
<p>Abstract</p>
<p>The role of the bystander is not one that is easily understood in the anti-bullying literature. Roles within the unofficial hierarchy of the school-yard and playground overlap considerably, and each role has its own social dynamic that brings with it a shifting behavioral landscape that affects every student. In this article, the mental health correlates of three categories of bystander are explored: the co-victim, the isolate, and the confederate. Each category of bystander has its own characterizations and mental health correlates. Reports of post-traumatic stress, internalized hostility, substance use, and suicide ideation are discussed with reference to studies involving witnesses of family abuse, community and school violence as well as bullying. It is argued that bystanders are the key to challenging bullying in schools, and their mental health and well-being is pivotal to the effectiveness of anti-bullying interventions.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
					
					<wfw:commentRss>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-on-the-health-impact-of-being-a-bystander/feed/</wfw:commentRss>

		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">17366</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>New research: Does bullying lead to dating aggression?</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-does-bullying-lead-to-dating-aggression/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/09/new-research-does-bullying-lead-to-dating-aggression/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Alexis Marbach]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 10 Sep 2012 15:35:08 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Resources]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[bullying]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[dating violence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[resources]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">http://www.preventconnect.org/?p=17365</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[&#8220;Bullying: a stepping stone towards dating aggression?&#8221; examines the ways in which the risk factors and  bullying and dating aggression&#160;&#8230;]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<p><a href="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/Screen-shot-2012-09-10-at-9.34.49-AM.png?x47887"><img loading="lazy" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-17375" title="Screen shot 2012-09-10 at 9.34.49 AM" src="http://www.preventconnect.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/09/Screen-shot-2012-09-10-at-9.34.49-AM-150x150.png?x47887" alt="" width="150" height="150" /></a>&#8220;<a href="http://www.degruyter.com/view/j/ijamh.2012.24.issue-1/ijamh.2012.006/ijamh.2012.006.xml">Bullying: a stepping stone towards dating aggression?</a>&#8221; examines the ways in which the risk factors and  bullying and dating aggression are similar and ways in which a young person could exhibit both acts of power and control over the course of the youth and adolescence. The  article describes the shared key fundamental elements and roots causes of both behaviors such creating a power differential within interpersonal relationships and what Albert Bandura calls &#8220;moral disengagement&#8221;. Moral disengagement is defined as, &#8220;a lack of self regulation regarding moral conduct&#8221;. While there is not a direct causal link between bullying and dating aggression later in life, researchers point out that:<span id="more-17365"></span></p>
<blockquote><p>&#8230;young adolescents who perpetrate bullying become involved in romantic relationships earlier than those who do not bully, and are more likely to report verbal and physical aggression in their earliest intimate relationships. <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909910">(Josephson and Pepler, 2012)</a></p></blockquote>
<p>Additionally, the authors site five features that bullying and dating agression prevention programs should share: focus on relationships, emphasis on skill building, focus on peer contexts, a comprehensive approach, and focus on the issue of power.</p>
<p>There is still much research to be done to further explain the relationship between these two forms of abuse but this article sheds light on shared risk factors and potential mediators and moderators, helping to inform the work of preventionists.</p>
<p>To learn more: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909910"><!--more--></a></p>
<blockquote><p><a href="http://www.degruyter.com/view/j/ijamh.2012.24.issue-1/ijamh.2012.006/ijamh.2012.006.xml"><strong>Bullying: a stepping stone to dating aggression?</strong></a></p>
<p>Josephson WL, Pepler D.<a title="International journal of adolescent medicine and health." href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22909910#">, International Journal of Adolescent Medicine and Health.</a> 2011 Nov 29;24(1):37-47.</p></blockquote>
<div>
<blockquote><p><strong>Abstract</strong></p>
<p>Bullying is the use of power and aggression to control and distress another. In this paper, we review research to explore whether the lessons learned in bullying provide a stepping stone to aggressive behavior in dating relationships. We start by considering definitions and a relationship framework with which to understand both bullying and dating aggression. We consider bullying from a developmental-contextual perspective and consider risk factors associated with the typical developmental patterns for bullying and dating aggression, including developmental and sociodemographic, individual attributes, and family, peer group, community, and societal relationship contexts that might lead some children and youths to follow developmental pathways that lead to bullying and dating aggression. We conclude by discussing implications for intervention with a review of evidence-based interventions.</p></blockquote>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<blockquote><p><em><strong>The Higher Education Center&#8217;s </strong></em><em><strong>Prevention Update: Bystander Intervention</strong></em><strong><em>, </em>Now Posted</strong></p>
<p>According to the <a href="http://www.nsvrc.org/">National Sexual Violence Resource Center</a>, bystander intervention programs teach potential witnesses safe and positive ways that they can act to prevent or intervene when there is a risk for sexual violence. &#8220;A bystander approach gives community members specific roles that they can use in preventing sexual violence, including naming and stopping situations before they happen, stepping in during an incident, and speaking out against ideas and behaviors that support sexual violence. This approach develops skills to be an effective and supportive ally to survivors after an assault has taken place.&#8221;</p>

		<title>Social Media, Social Life: How Teens View Their Digital Lives</title>
		<link>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/07/social-media-social-life-how-teens-views-their-social-lives/</link>
					<comments>http://www.preventconnect.org/2012/07/social-media-social-life-how-teens-views-their-social-lives/#comments</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Ashley Maier]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 09 Jul 2012 19:25:55 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Blog]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[prevention]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social media]]></category>
